Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
The Four Causes for Abandoning Literal Meanings (Haqa'iq al-Alfaz)
Lists four causes to abandon literal meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four causes that override the literal meaning of a word in Hanafi usul and apply them to derive rulings from Quran and Hadith.

Core Idea
Literal meaning is default, but context, custom, reason, or speaker's state can override it.

In this passage from Usul al-Shashi, the author explains that the literal meaning (haqiqah) of legal texts is not always binding. Four indicators can cause the literal meaning to be abandoned: context of speech (siyaq al-kalam), custom (al-urf), rational proof (dalil 'aqli), and an indication from the speaker's prior character or state (dalil min qabl al-mutakallim). Each cause is illustrated with a concrete example from Shariah, showing how the Hanafi school derives nuanced rulings by considering these indicators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
Recognize that the literal meaning (haqiqah) is the starting point for interpreting any legal text. The passage lists four exceptions where this default is abandoned.
Hanafi usul uses the principle 'al-haqiqah tu'tabaru ma lam taqum al-qarinah' (literal meaning is considered unless a counter-indicator arises).
2
Examine the first cause: Siyaq al-Kalam (Context of Speech)
Study the example of the hadith of the fly: The Prophet (ﷺ) commanded to dip the fly into the drink. The literal command suggests obligation, but the context ('the fly presents disease before medicine') indicates it is a recommendation for our benefit, not a devotional obligation.
Context includes surrounding words, situation, and purpose.
3
Examine the second cause: Al-Urf (Custom) and Siyaq al-Kalam combined
Analyze the zakat verse (9:60). The literal form (lam of entitlement, waw of conjunction) suggests all eight categories must be given. However, the preceding verse (9:58) provides context: hypocrites criticized distribution. The Hanafi position says the listing is merely to explain eligible categories, not to mandate giving to every category.
This example also shows overlap between causes (siyaq and urf). The custom of the Arabs in using such structures supports the non-literal interpretation.
4
Examine the third cause: Dalil 'Aqli (Rational Proof)
Consider the verse 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' (18:29). Literally, this is a command to disbelieve, which is impossible from the Wise Lawgiver. Rational proof (that God does not command evil) overrides the literal imperative to a threat or permission.
This cause relies on the attribute of the Lawgiver (hikmah, wisdom).
5
Examine the fourth cause: Dalil min Qabl al-Mutakallim (Speaker's Prior Indication)
Apply the agency example: If a principal says 'buy meat' and he is a traveler on the road, his state (need for immediate food) indicates he means cooked meat, not raw. The literal absolute is restricted by the speaker's circumstances.
This cause is linked to the condition of the speaker, not the text itself.

Source Text

(۱) قوله: [وإنه ليقدم... إلخ] أي: ريشاً فيه داء على ريش الدواء، قال العلماء: معنى الداء في أحد جناحيه الكبر والترفع عن استباحة ما أباحه الشارع، فإنّ الشرع أباح الطعام بموت ما ليس له دم سائل والإنسان إذا استبعد ذلك ترفعاً ورمى به كبراً، فقد أضاع نعم الله تعالى، ثُمّ إذا غمس كره النفس على استباحة ما أباحه الشارع، فيكون قاهراً أي: غالباً على هواها أتم القهر. (۲) قوله: [فلا يكون للإيجاب] الّذي هو حقيقة الأمر بل هو أمر شفقة ورحمة؛ لأنّ منفعته عائدة إلينا، فيكون نظراً في حقنا لا في حق الشرع، كذا في "المعدن". (۳) قوله: [عقیب... آه] فالوقوع عقيبه وتعقيبه تفريعه قرينة على أنّ المراد أنّ المصارف هي هذه الأصناف إليهم كان لا تلك المنافقون الطامعون في الصدقات، فالمراد بيان أصنافهم في صلوح المصرفية لا بيان الاستحقاق، كما يقال: «الخلافة لقريش» و «السقاية لبني هاشم، واستدل له صاحب "الهداية بالإضافة من أنها للبيان أنهم مصارف لا للاستحقاق، وهذا لما عرف أن الزكاة حق الله تعالى، وبعلة الفقر صاروا مصارف فلا يبالي باختلاف جهاتهم... إلخ. (٤) قوله: [يدل على أنّ... إلخ] فإنه وإن كان حقيقة الآية وجوب الصرف إليهم وإلى الثلاثة من كل صنف، كما ذهب إليه الشافعي رحمه الله لإضافة الصدقة إليهم بلام الاستحقاق، وهم مذكورون بواو الجمع فكانت الصدقة لجميعهم، لكنه تركت حقيقته بدلالة سياق الكلام، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمِنْهُم مَّن يَلْمِرُكَ فِي الصَّدَقَاتِ فَإِنْ أَعْطُواْ مِنْهَا أي: من الصدقات بمقتضى طبايعهم ﴿رَضُواْ وَإِن لَّمْ يُعْطَوْاْ مِنهَا إِذَا هُمْ يَسْخَطُونَ ﴾ [التوبة : ٥٨]، فإنّ هذه الآية إلى آخرها تدلّ على أن ذكر الأصناف يقطع طمعهم من الصدقات ببيان المصارف لها فلا يتوقف الخروج عن العهدة على الأداء إلى الكل، والرابع: قد تترك الحقيقة بدلالة من قبل المتكلم، مثالـه قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن شَاء فَلْيُؤْمِن وَمَن شَاء فَلْيَكْفُرْ [الكهف: ٢٩]؛ وذلك أي كون ترك الحقيقة بدلالة من قبل المتكلم ثابتا في الآية. لأنّ الله تعالى حكيم والكفر قبيح والحكيم لا يأمر به، فيترك دلالة اللفظ على الأمر بحكمة الأمر، وعلى هذا قلنا: إذا وكل بشراء اللحم فإن كان الموكل مسافرا نزل على الطريق فهو على المطبوخ أو على المشوي وإن كان صاحب منزل فهو على النيّ. (۱) قوله: [بدلالة من قبل المتكلّم وشانه مطلقاً أو في حال المتكلّم مطلقاً أو مع معاضدة القرائن الحالية كما في يمين وكمسألة التغذي من وقوع الطلب أو إرادة الخروج، كذا في "الفصول". (۲) قوله: [ فَمَن شَاءَ ... إلخ] اعلم أنه يظهر لك بعد التعمق والنظر في أمثال هذا المثال أنّ هذه الأقسام والأنحاء لترك الحقيقة قد تتداخل وتجتمع بعضها مع بعض في كثير من الأمثلة، كهذا المثال فإنه يصلح أن يقال: إنّه تركت فيه الحقيقة :أي: الإباحة للكفر المفهومة من الأمر أو وجوبه أو ندبه بدلالة العرف والاستعمال. (۳) قوله: [بحكمة الآمر على صيغة الفاعل، ويحمل الأمر على التوبيخ؛ لأنه ضده لما ذكرنا أنّ الأمر لإتيان المأمور به والتوبيخ لإعدامه، كذا في "المعدن". (٤) قوله: [وعلى هذا] أي: على أنّ الحقيقة قد تترك بدلالة معنى يرجع إلى المتكلّم، كذا في "المعدن". (5) قوله: [فهو على الني] أي: على غير المطبوخ، فإنّ حقيقة هذا التوكيل شراء مطلق اللحم في الصورتين، لكن ترك إطلاقه فيهما بدلالة حاله، وهو أنه إذا نزل على الطريق فحاله يدل على أنه يطلب اللحم ليتغذى به فيصرف ذلك إلى المهيا للأكل حتى لو اشترى التي يكون مشترياً لنفسه لا ...
Chapter on what causes the abandonment of literal meanings. i.e., the wing. i.e., the fly. i.e., a feather. A feather with medicine. Our statement: we said. And in the other wing there is medicine, and it (the hadith) states that it (the fly) presents the disease before the medicine; the context of the speech indicates that the command (to dip the fly) is for... i.e., it does not contain the meaning of glorifying his Lord Almighty. The command regarding the (dipped) fly. The (dipped fly) is to repel harm from us, not for a devotional command as a right of the Shariah, so it is not for obligation. And His statement Almighty: 'Indeed the charities are for the poor' (9:60) following His statement: 'And among them are those who criticize you concerning the charities' (9:58) indicates that mentioning the categories is for... Our statement: we said in His statement: and upon this we said by means of the conjunction. The harm from us not for a devotional command truly for the Shariah, so it is not for obligation; because it is for us, not against us; because the purpose of the command is only trial and testing, and that is not achieved in requesting what benefits the servants. But there is an objection: because its being beneficial does not negate obligation and commitment; because it is possible that Allah Almighty charges His servant with what benefits the servant as reform of his body. Do you not see His statement: 'And do not throw yourselves into destruction' (2:195), and for this we said: eating beyond satiety is forbidden, and many examples like this, as in 'al-Sharh'. (1) His statement: 'and it presents... etc.' i.e., a feather with disease over a feather of medicine. The scholars said: the meaning of the disease in one of its wings is arrogance and self-elevation from deeming permissible what the Lawgiver has permitted. Indeed the Shariah permitted food by the death of what has no flowing blood, and when a person deems that remote out of arrogance and throws it away as pride, he has wasted the blessings of Allah Almighty. Then when he dips (the fly), he compels his soul to deem permissible what the Shariah permitted, so he becomes a subduer, i.e., dominant over his desire completely. (2) His statement: 'so it is not for obligation' which is the literal meaning of the command, rather it is a command of compassion and mercy; because its benefit returns to us, so it is a consideration for our right, not for the right of the Shariah, as in 'al-Ma'din'. (3) His statement: 'following... etc.' So its occurrence after it and its conjunction and branching is a contextual clue that the intended meaning is that the recipients are these categories, not those hypocrites who covet the charities. So the intended meaning is to explain their categories as suitable for being recipients, not to explain entitlement, as it is said: 'The caliphate is for Quraysh' and 'the watering is for Banu Hashim'. The author of 'al-Hidayah' used as evidence the annexation (idafah) that it is for explanation (bayān) that they are recipients, not for entitlement. And this is based on the known fact that zakat is the right of Allah Almighty, and by the cause of poverty they become recipients, so he does not care about the difference of their directions... etc. (4) His statement: 'indicates that... etc.' For although the literal meaning of the verse is the obligation of disbursing to them and to the three from every category, as Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) held, due to the annexation of charity to them with the lam of entitlement, and they are mentioned with the conjunction waw, so the charity is for all of them. However, its literal meaning is abandoned due to the context of the speech, namely His statement: 'And among them are those who criticize you concerning the charities. If they are given from them, i.e., from the charities according to their natures, they become content; but if they are not given from them, behold, they become angry' (9:58). This verse to its end indicates that mentioning the categories cuts off their covetousness from the charities by explaining the recipients for them, so release from liability does not depend on payment to all. And the fourth: the literal meaning may be abandoned due to an indication from the speaker. Example: His statement Almighty: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' (18:29). That is because Allah Almighty is Wise, and disbelief is ugly, and the Wise does not command it. So the literal indication of the wording is abandoned in favor of a wise command. And upon this we said: If someone appoints an agent to buy meat, if the principal is a traveler stopping on the road, the agent is to buy cooked or grilled meat; but if he is a host at home, the agent is to buy raw meat. (1) His statement: 'by an indication from the speaker and his state absolutely, or in the state of the speaker absolutely, or with the support of circumstantial clues as in an oath and as in the question of feeding from the occurrence of the request or the desire for exit, as in 'al-Fusul'. (2) His statement: 'So whoever wills... etc.' Know that after deep thought and consideration in examples like this, it appears to you that these categories and types for abandoning the literal meaning may overlap and combine with each other in many examples, like this example. For it is suitable to say that the literal meaning is abandoned in it, i.e., the permissibility of disbelief understood from the command, or its obligation, or its recommendation, by the indication of custom and usage. (3) His statement: 'by the wisdom of the commander in the active participle form, and the command is interpreted as a rebuke; because it is the opposite because we mentioned that the command is for performing the commanded act and rebuke is for omitting it, as in 'al-Ma'din'. (4) His statement: 'and upon this' i.e., that the literal meaning may be abandoned due to a meaning that goes back to the speaker, as in 'al-Ma'din'. (5) His statement: 'so it is for raw' i.e., not cooked. For the literal meaning of this agency is the absolute purchase of meat in both situations, but its absoluteness is abandoned in both cases by the indication of his condition. For when he stops on the road, his condition indicates that he seeks meat to eat, so it is directed to what is prepared for eating, even if he buys what is for himself, not...
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi defines the principle that the literal meaning (haqiqah) of words may be abandoned in favor of a non-literal meaning when certain indicators are present. The author presents four causes: (1) siyaq al-kalam (context of speech), (2) al-'urf (custom), (3) dalil 'aqli (rational proof), and (4) dalil min qabl al-mutakallim (an indication from the speaker's prior character or state). The first cause is illustrated by the hadith of the fly: the Prophet's command to dip the fly in the drink is not an obligatory command (wujub) but a recommendation, because the context shows it is for our benefit, not a devotional act. The second cause is shown by the verse on zakat recipients (9:60). Although the literal form of the verse with the lam of entitlement and the conjunction waw suggests that all eight categories must receive, the surrounding context (9:58) shows that the polytheists were criticizing the distribution; thus the listing is merely to inform that these are the eligible categories, not that each must be given. This is the Hanafi position against the Shafi'i view that all categories must be included. The third cause is rational: a command that seems harmful may be interpreted as a threat rather than an obligation, as in 'So whoever wills, let him believe...' (18:29). Since God is Wise and would not command disbelief, the literal imperative is abandoned for a meaning of threat or permission. The fourth cause is the speaker's state: if an agent is told to buy meat, a traveler's need for ready-to-eat food indicates he means cooked meat, while a host at home means raw meat. This shows that the literal absolute meaning is qualified by the speaker's circumstances. The commentator elaborates that these categories can overlap, and the examples demonstrate the application of usul principles in deriving rulings.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as established by language.
سياق الكلام (siyaq al-kalam): The context of speech; surrounding words and situation that clarify intended meaning.
بدلالة من قبل المتكلم (bi-dalālah min qabl al-mutakallim): An indication from the speaker's prior character or state that overrides the literal meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason the Hanafi school does not interpret the zakat verse (9:60) as requiring distribution to all eight categories?
A) The verse is abrogated by another verse.
B) The word 'li' (for) is always for bayān (explanation) and never for istihqāq.
C) The context of the verse (9:58) provides a strong indicator that the listing is explanatory, not obligatory for each category.
D) The eight categories do not exist in modern times.

Key Takeaway
The literal meaning is the default, but four strong indicators—context, custom, rational proof, and the speaker's state—can legitimately override it in Hanafi usul, allowing rulings to align with the Lawgiver's intent.

Abandoning Literal Meaning by Speaker's Indication
Defines the first type of abandoning literal meaning: speaker's indication with yamin al-fawr example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a speaker's own indication (dalala min qibal al-mutakallim) narrows a general expression to a specific referent, and apply this to the case of yamin al-fawr.

Core Idea
A general oath can be limited to a specific instance by the speaker's own contextual indication.

This lesson introduces a key concept in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: literal meanings (haqa'iq) are sometimes abandoned in favor of contextual meanings. The first type of such abandonment is triggered by the speaker's own indication (dalala min qibal al-mutakallim). Understanding this principle is essential for correctly interpreting oaths and other expressions that appear general but are tied to a specific situation. We will explore the classic example of the 'oath of immediate action' (yamin al-fawr).

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Expression
Look at the oath: 'La ataghadda' (I will not eat lunch). The term 'al-taghaddi' (lunch) is indefinite and could refer to any lunch. This is the literal or general meaning.
In Arabic, the absence of a definite article or qualifier typically indicates generality.
2
Recognize the Speaker's Contextual Indication
The oath was uttered immediately after an invitation: 'Come eat lunch with me.' The speaker's response is directly connected to that specific invitation. This connection provides a contextual indication (qarina) that the oath is about the invited lunch only.
The 'indication of the speaker' (dalala al-mutakallim) is the implicit reference created by the conversational situation.
3
Apply the Principle of Abandoning Literal Meaning
Because of the contextual indication, the literal generality of 'la ataghadda' is abandoned and restricted to the specific lunch invitation. This is an application of the rule that haqiqa can be left when there is a clear contextual clue (qarina).
Hanafis recognize this as a valid form of takhsis (specificiation) without needing an explicit text.
4
Derive the Ruling for the Oath
The consequence is that the oath is not broken if the person eats lunch elsewhere that day, or even the same lunch later at home, because the oath only applied to the invited meal. If he eats lunch with the inviter, that would break the oath.
This ruling flows directly from the restricted scope of the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِيمَا يُتْرَكُ بِهِ حَقَائِقُ الْأَلْفَاظِ. وَمِنْ هَذَا النَّوْعِ يَمِينُ الْفَوْرِ. مِثَالُهُ: إِذَا قَالَ: تَعَالَ تَغَدَّ مَعِي فَقَالَ: وَاللَّهِ لَا أَتَغَدَّى. فَذَلِكَ يَنْصَرِفُ إِلَى الْغَدَاءِ الْمَدْعُوِّ إِلَيْهِ حَتَّى لَوْ تَغَدَّى بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ فِي مَنْزِلِهِ مَعَهُ أَوْ مَعَ غَيْرِهِ فِي ذَلِكَ الْيَوْمِ لَا يَحْنَثُ. وَالْخَامِسُ: قَدْ تُتْرَكُ الْحَقِيقَةُ بِدَلَالَةِ مَحَلِّ الْكَلَامِ.
Chapter on what causes the abandonment of literal meanings of words. And from this type is the oath of immediate action. Its example: if someone says: 'Come eat lunch with me' and he says: 'By Allah, I will not eat lunch,' then that is directed to the lunch to which he was invited, so that if he later eats lunch in his home with him or with someone else on that day, he does not break his oath. And the fifth: sometimes literal meaning is abandoned by the indication of the context of the speech.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on the abandonment of literal meanings (haqa'iq) in favor of metaphorical or contextual meanings. The author introduces the first type: abandonment by the speaker's own indication (dalala min qibal al-mutakallim). The example given is the 'oath of immediate action' (yamin al-fawr). A man is invited to lunch; he swears 'I will not eat lunch.' The literal meaning of 'la ataghadda' is general, but the speaker's context (the invitation) narrows it to the specific lunch. If he eats lunch elsewhere, he does not break his oath. The text then briefly mentions a fifth type (abandonment by context of speech), which will be elaborated later.

Key Terms
يَمِينُ الْفَوْرِ (yamin al-fawr): An oath confined to the immediate situation due to the speaker's indication.
التَّغَدِّي (al-taghaddi): Eating the morning meal (lunch) up to midday.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of yamin al-fawr, if someone swears 'La ataghadda' after being invited, when does he break his oath?
A) If he eats any lunch that day.
B) If he eats the specific lunch to which he was invited.
C) If he eats lunch at any time.
D) If he eats lunch with the inviter only.

Key Takeaway
The speaker's own contextual indication can narrow a general oath to a specific referent, as seen in yamin al-fawr.

Two Key Textual Implications: 'Ibarah and 'Isharah
Definition of textual implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'ibarah and isharah and state how each is derived from the text.

Core Idea
'Ibarah is the explicit primary meaning; 'Isharah is a secondary implication from the text's arrangement.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces two fundamental types of textual implication (dalalah). Understanding these is critical for deriving rulings from Quran and Sunnah. The text also mentions two more types (dalalah and iqtida'), but this lesson focuses on the first two.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types listed
The text lists four: 'ibarah, isharah, dalalah, and iqtida'. Focus on the first two for this lesson.
The other two will be covered separately.
2
Define 'ibarah
The text says it is 'ما سيق الكلام لأجله وأريد به قصدا' – the meaning for which the speech was brought and intended primarily. This is the clearest form of textual evidence.
3
Define isharah
The text says it is 'ما ثبت بنظم النص من غير زيادة' – established by the arrangement (nazm) of the text without any addition. It is not apparent from every aspect.
4
Contrast the two
The main difference: 'ibarah is primary and intended; isharah is secondary and derived from the logical structure of the text. For example, from the verse 'فلا تقل لهما أف' (17:23), the 'ibarah forbids saying 'uf', while the isharah prohibits any form of harm to parents because the gradation of severity implies it.
5
Apply to a new verse
Read a verse like (2:275) 'وأحل الله البيع وحرم الربا'. The 'ibarah declares trade permissible and riba forbidden. The isharah might indicate that any transaction resembling riba is also prohibited because the prohibition is based on the underlying illah of excess.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في متعلقات النصوص نعني بها : عبارة النص وإشارته ودلالته واقتضاؤه، فأما 'عبارة النص فهو ما سيق الكلام لأجله وأريد به قصدا، وأمّا إشارة النص فهي ما ثبت بنظم النص من غير زيادة و هو غير ظاهر من كل وجه، مثاله هي ...
Chapter on the derivatives of the texts: we mean by them: the explicit statement of the text, its indication, its implication, and its requirement. As for the explicit statement of the text, it is that for which the speech was brought and intended primarily. As for the indication of the text, it is what is established by the arrangement of the text without any addition, and it is not apparent from every aspect. Its example is ...
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces the four types of textual implications (mutallaqat al-nusus): 'ibarah (explicit statement), isharah (indication), dalalah (implicit meaning), and iqtida' (requirement). The focus here is on the first two. 'Ibarah is the primary meaning for which the text was revealed; it is the clear, intended ruling. Ishara is a meaning that is understood from the wording of the text but was not the primary purpose; it is based on the logical arrangement of the words and does not require adding anything to the text. The text notes that isharah is not apparent from every angle (ghayr zahir min kulli wajh), meaning it is less obvious than 'ibarah but still valid.

Key Terms
عبارة النص (ibarat al-nass): The explicit meaning for which the text was brought and intended primarily.
إشارة النص (isharat al-nass): A meaning established by the arrangement (nazm) of the text without any additional wording, and it is not apparent from every aspect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes 'ibarah from isharah?
A) 'Ibarah requires addition to the text, isharah does not.
B) 'Ibarah is the primary intended meaning; isharah is established from the arrangement of the text without addition.
C) 'Ibarah is based on necessity; isharah is based on indication.
D) 'Ibarah is apparent from every aspect; isharah is not apparent at all.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between 'ibarah and isharah is foundational: 'ibarah is the intended primary meaning, isharah is an implied secondary meaning from the text's structure.

Deriving Rulings from Quranic Implication (Isharah)
Deriving a ruling from Quranic implication (isharah)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi concept of dalalah bi al-isharah to extract a ruling from a Quranic text, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the principle of literal meaning (haqiqah).

Core Idea
Text's necessary implication (isharah) is a valid source of legal ruling in Hanafi usul, provided it relies on literal meaning and necessary consequence.

This lesson explores a powerful tool in Hanafi usul: how a Quranic description can imply a legal ruling without explicit command. The passage focuses on the verse about the poor emigrants (Surah al-Hashr 59:8), demonstrating how the Hanafi school derives that a disbeliever's seizure of Muslim property transfers ownership. You will learn the logical chain from text to ruling, and the debate with the Shafi'i school over literal vs metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Source Text and Its Explicit Purpose
The verse is part of Surah al-Hashr and was revealed to specify recipients of war booty (fay'). Recognize it as a Quranic text with qati' thubut but zanni dalala regarding the specific implication.
The verse is primarily about distribution of spoils, but the Hanafi author focuses on the description 'fuqara'.
2
Extract the Literal Meaning (Haqiqah) of Key Term
The term 'fuqara' (poor) literally means those who lack ownership of property. The Hanafi position insists on taking this literal meaning unless there is a compelling reason to shift to metaphor.
This step applies the usul principle: al-asl fi al-kalam al-haqiqah.
3
Connect to Contextual Evidence
The same verse says they were driven out of 'their homes and properties' (amwalihim). This proves they owned property before migration. Therefore, calling them 'poor' only makes sense if their ownership of those properties has ended.
This establishes that their poverty is acquired, not original.
4
Apply the Necessary Consequence (Lazim)
If their ownership has ended, the property cannot be ownerless (la dhiya' fi al-Islam). The necessary consequence is that ownership must have transferred to the disbelievers who seized it. This is the core of isharah: a ruling derived from implication.
The Hanafi author argues that what follows necessarily from the text is attached to it.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i Reading and Derive the Ruling
The Shafi'i school considers 'fuqara' metaphorical, meaning 'those who left their wealth', so ownership does not transfer. The Hanafi response is that the default is literal meaning; metaphor is not adopted without necessity. The derived ruling: when a disbeliever seizes Muslim property and secures it in dar al-harb, it becomes the disbeliever's property.
This leads to branches: the merchant who buys from him owns it, and the Muslim warrior who captures it owns it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في متعلقات النصوص
في قوله تعالى: {لِلْفُقَرَاءِ الْمُهَاجِرِينَ الَّذِينَ أُخْرِجُوا مِن دِيَارِهِمْ} [الحشر: ۸] الآية. فإنه سيق لبيان استحقاق الغنيمة فصار نصا في ذلك، وقد ثبت فقرهم بنظم النص وهو قوله تعالى: {لِلْفُقَرَاءِ الْمُهَاجِرِينَ}، فكان إشارة إلى أن استيلاء الكافر على مال المسلم سبب لثبوت الملك للكافر؛ إذ لو كانت الأموال باقية على ملكهم لا يثبت فقرهم. ولا يقال: إن إطلاق الفقراء عليهم باعتبار العدم الأصلي؛ لأنه قد كانت لهم أموال بمكة بدليل قوله تعالى: {الَّذِينَ أُخْرِجُوا مِن دِيَارِهِمْ وَأَمْوَالِهِمْ}. فإن قلت: ليس في النص تصريح بأن استيلاء الكفار سبب لثبوت الملك، فكيف يكون إشارة إليه؟ قلت: لما ثبت زوال أملاكهم بإشارته، ومن لوازم زوال الملك ثبوت الملك للكافرين الذين استولوا عليها؛ لأنه لا ضياع في الإسلام، فكان ما ثبت من لوازم الإشارة ملحقاً بها. والشافعي رحمه الله لم يعمل بهذه الإشارة قائلاً: بأن الله تعالى سماهم فقراء مجازاً لتركهم أموالهم. ونقول: صرف الكلام إلى المجاز مع إمكان العمل بالحقيقة خلاف الأصل فلا يصار إليه من غير ضرورة. ويخرج منه الحكم في مسئلة الاستيلاء: أن الكافر إذا استولى على مال المسلم فأحرزه في دار الحرب صار ملكاً له عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي. وكذلك حكم ثبوت الملك للتاجر بالشراء منهم، وتصرفاته من البيع والهبة والإعتاق، وحكم ثبوت الملك للغازي وعجز المالك عن انتزاعه من يده، وتفريعاته. ومثال الإشارة: كما إذا رأى إنسان إنسانا بقصد نظره، ومع ذلك يرى من كان عن يمينه وشماله بمؤق عينيه من غير التفات وقصد، فالأول بمنزلة العبارة والثاني بمنزلة الإشارة.
Chapter on Appurtenances of Texts. Regarding the verse: 'For the poor emigrants who were driven from their homes' [59:8]. It was brought to explain the entitlement to spoils, so it became a text (nass) for that. And their poverty is established by the wording of the text – His statement 'for the poor emigrants'. So it was an indication (isharah) that the seizure of a Muslim's property by a disbeliever is a cause for the establishment of ownership for the disbeliever; because if the properties remained in their ownership, their poverty would not be established. It cannot be said that calling them poor is due to original lack of wealth, because they had wealth in Mecca as evidenced by His statement 'who were driven from their homes and properties'. If you say: The text does not explicitly state that the disbelievers' seizure is a cause for ownership, how is it an indication? I say: Since their ownership's removal is established by indication, and from the necessary consequences of the removal of ownership is the establishment of ownership for the disbelievers who seized them, because there is no loss in Islam, so what is established from the necessary consequences of the indication is attached to it. Al-Shafi'i did not act upon this indication, saying that Allah called them poor metaphorically because they left their wealth. And we say: Turning speech to metaphor while it is possible to act upon the literal meaning is contrary to the principle, so it is not resorted to without necessity. And from this is derived the ruling in the matter of seizure: that if a disbeliever seizes a Muslim's property and secures it in the abode of war, it becomes his property according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. Likewise the ruling of ownership for the merchant who buys from them, and his transactions of sale, gift, and manumission, and the ruling of ownership for the warrior and the owner's inability to reclaim it from his hand, and its branches. An example of indication: just as when a person sees someone with intent and focus, yet he also sees those to his right and left with the corner of his eye without turning and intent, the first is like explicit expression (ibarah) and the second like indication (isharah).
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the concept of 'dalalah bi al-isharah' (indication by implication) in Hanafi usul. The chapter begins by defining that texts have not only explicit meanings (ibarah) but also implied significations (isharah). The author uses the Quranic verse about the poor Muhajireen to demonstrate how a ruling can be derived from implication. The verse was revealed to specify the recipients of war booty, but by calling them 'poor' it implies that they no longer own their former properties, because true poverty is lack of ownership. From this implication, the Hanafis deduce that the disbelievers who seized those properties became their owners, since property cannot be ownerless. The passage then addresses a potential objection: perhaps they were called poor because they left their wealth behind, not because they lost ownership. This is refuted by citing another part of the verse stating they were driven out of their 'properties', indicating they had owned property. The commentary clarifies that the indication is based on necessary consequence (lazim). The Shafi'i objection is presented: they consider the term 'poor' metaphorical, not literal. The Hanafi response is that the default is literal meaning (haqiqah), and one cannot shift to metaphor without necessity. The derived rulings include: (1) a disbeliever who seizes Muslim property in dar al-harb becomes its owner (contrary to Shafi'i), (2) a Muslim merchant who buys such property from the disbelievers also acquires ownership, and (3) a Muslim warrior who captures it becomes owner with full disposal rights. The passage concludes with an analogy: explicit expression is like looking directly at someone, while implication is like seeing someone peripherally without turning your head.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-isharah): The implied meaning of a text that is not explicitly stated but necessarily follows from its wording or context.
النص (al-nass): A clear textual statement that explicitly indicates a ruling.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word according to its linguistic convention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul discussed, why could the disbeliever's seizure be considered a cause for ownership?
A) Because the verse explicitly states that disbelievers become owners.
B) Because calling the emigrants 'poor' implies loss of ownership, and property cannot be ownerless.
C) Because the Shafi'i school agreed with this conclusion.
D) Because of general consensus among all madhahib.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a text's necessary implication (isharah) carries legal weight, especially when the literal meaning and logical consequence align.

Dalalat al-Nass: Linguistic Indication in Hanafi Usul
Defining dalalat al-nass and deriving rulings on fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define dalalat al-nass, distinguish it from qiyas, and apply it to derive the Hanafi ruling on intention for fasting.

Core Idea
Dalalat al-nass is linguistic certainty: the text's wording itself reveals the cause, no deduction needed.

In Hanafi usul, not all legal derivations are created equal. Some are directly read from the words of the text — these are called dalalat al-nass (indication of the text). This lesson introduces this concept and shows how it produces certain rulings on fasting that differ sharply from those based on qiyas or isolated hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the definition of dalalat al-nass in the passage: it is what is known as the 'illa (effective cause) of a ruling from the text linguistically, not by ijtihad or deduction.
The key is 'lughatan' (linguistically) — no human reasoning is involved.
2
Understand the condition
The indication must be immediate from the wording; if it requires ijtihad or qiyas, it is not dalalat al-nass. This is the boundary with qiyas.
3
Observe the first application: janaba does not break the fast
The verse 'then complete the fast until night' implies the fast has already begun validly. Therefore, janaba (major impurity) at dawn does not prevent the existence of the fast, so it cannot invalidate it.
This is pure dalalat al-nass: the command to complete presupposes a valid start.
4
Trace further rulings derived by extension
From the ruling on janaba, the text extends to madmada, istinshaq, tasting, wet dream, cupping, and oiling — all do not invalidate because they do not affect the fast's initial validity.
This is takyif (analogical extension) but based on the same linguistic logic.
5
Analyze the dispute on night intention (tabyit)
The same verse yields that the command to complete attaches only after the first part of the day. Hence intention is not required from the night; it can be made after dawn. Contrast with the Shafi'i view based on a hadith.
The conflict is between dalalat al-nass (Quranic linguistic indication) and a solitary hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في متعلقات النصوص
أي من كون الجنابة لا تنافي الصوم. بإتمامه فكان هذا إشارة إلى أن الجنابة لا تنافي الصوم، ولزم من ذلك أن المضمضة والاستنشاق لا ينافي بقاء الصوم، ويتفرع منه أن من ذاق شيئا ولم يجاوز حلقه بفمه لم يفسد صومه فإنه لو كان الماء مالحا يجد طعمه عند المضمضة لا يفسد به الصوم، وعلم منه حكم الاحتلام والاحتجام والادهان؛ لأنّ فكل واحد منها لا يفسد الصوم. الكتاب لَمّا سمى الإمساك اللازم بواسطة الانتهاء عن الأشياء الثلاثة في النص وهي الأكل والشرب والجماع. فصل في متعلقات النصوص المذكورة في أول الصبح صوما علم أن ركن الصوم يتم بالانتهاء عن الأشياء الثلاثة، وعلى هذا يخرّج الحكم في مسئلة التبييت فإن قصد الإتيان بالمأمور به إنما يلزمه عند توجّه الأمر والأمر إنّما يتوجّه بعد الجزء الأول لقوله تعالى: ﴿ ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى الَّليْلِ ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸۷]، وأما دلالة النص فهي ما علم علّة للحكم المنصوص عليه لغة لا اجتهادا ولا استنباطاً.
Chapter on the implications of texts. [Commentary: i.e., from the fact that major impurity does not negate fasting. By 'completing it' this was an indication that major impurity does not negate fasting, and from that it follows that rinsing the mouth and sniffing water do not negate the continuance of the fast, and it branches from that that one who tastes something and it does not pass his throat with his mouth, his fast is not invalidated, because if the water were salty and he tastes it when rinsing, it does not invalidate the fast. And from it the ruling on wet dream, cupping, and oiling is known, because each of them does not invalidate the fast. The author, when he named the necessary restraint by refraining from three things in the text – eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse. [Commentary continues:] Chapter on the implications of texts mentioned at the beginning of dawn as fasting – it is known that the pillar of fasting is completed by refraining from the three things. And upon this is derived the ruling in the issue of night intention: for the intention to perform the commanded act is only obligatory upon him when the command is directed, and the command is only directed after the first part [of the day] due to His saying: 'Then complete the fast until night' [Quran 2:187]. As for dalalat al-nass (indication of the text), it is what is known as the effective cause of a ruling from the text linguistically, not by ijtihad nor deduction.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi defines the concept of 'dalalat al-nass' (indication of the text) as a distinct category of legal inference. The author explains that dalalat al-nass is a meaning derived directly from the wording of the text through linguistic understanding, not through personal reasoning (ijtihad) or analogical deduction (qiyas). This is illustrated by several rulings related to fasting derived from the Quranic verse 'Then complete the fast until night' (2:187). The first ruling is that major impurity (janaba) does not invalidate the fast. The reasoning is that the command to complete the fast presupposes that the fast has already been validly begun; since a person in a state of janaba can validly begin a fast (as the fast is already valid at dawn), it follows that janaba does not negate the fast. From this, it is derived that rinsing the mouth (madmada) and sniffing water (istinshaq) during ghusl do not break the fast, since they are necessary for purification. Similarly, tasting food that does not reach the throat, using salty water that leaves a taste, wet dreams, cupping, and applying oil do not invalidate the fast. The second application is the issue of night intention (tabyit). The Hanafi position, based on dalalat al-nass, holds that a specific intention for fasting from the night before is not required; rather, the intention can be made after dawn, because the command to complete the fast implies that the command itself only becomes binding after the first part of the day has begun. This is contrasted with the Shafi'i view, which requires intention from the night based on a hadith. The passage emphasizes that dalalat al-nass is a linguistic, certain indication, unlike qiyas which is speculative.

Key Terms
دلالة النص (dalālat al-naṣṣ): The indication of the text that is known as the effective cause ('illa) for the ruling linguistically, not by ijtihad or deduction.
التبييت (al-tabyīt): The act of intending the fast from the night before.
الاحتلام (al-iḥtilām): Wet dream, which causes major impurity (janaba).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a wet dream (ihtilam) not invalidate the fast?
A) Because the fast is only invalidated by intentional eating or drinking.
B) Because the command to complete the fast implies the fast already exists, so janaba at the start does not prevent its validity.
C) Because the hadith says wet dream does not break the fast, overriding the Quran.
D) Because wet dream is not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Dalalat al-nass provides certain, linguistic rulings directly from the text, distinct from qiyas; it is used by Hanafis to derive, for example, that intention for fasting need not be made from the night.

The Ruling Revolves Around the 'Illah
Usul principle with illustrative cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the ruling revolves around the 'illah to derive rulings from Quranic verses and oaths.

Core Idea
The ruling exists when the cause exists and ceases when the cause ceases.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi explains a foundational Hanafi principle: the legal ruling is tied to its effective cause ('illah), not merely to the literal text. Through examples from the Quran, oaths, and daily transactions, the author demonstrates that the Shari'ah is based on meanings and purposes. Understanding this principle is key to correct ijtihad and distinguishing between literal and purposive interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Locate the core maxim: 'al-hukm yaduru ma'a al-'illah wujudan wa 'adaman' (the ruling revolves with the cause in existence and non-existence). This is the central thesis.
The principle is stated directly in the final sentence.
2
Examine the 'uff' example
The Quran prohibits saying 'uff' to parents (Quran 17:23). The Hanafi position is that the prohibition is due to the harm caused. If a people consider 'uff' as an honor (no harm), it is not prohibited. Thus, the 'illah (harm) drives the ruling.
This illustrates how the 'illah overrides the literal wording.
3
Apply to the Friday verse
Quran 62:9 commands leaving trade during the Friday call. Hanafis reason the 'illah is to attend prayer without hindrance. If a sale on a ship heading to the mosque does not hinder prayer, it is not disliked.
The ruling changes when the 'illah is absent.
4
Trace reasoning in oaths
Oaths are interpreted according to customary 'illah. For example, 'meat' means flesh generated from blood, so fish and locust are excluded. The 'illah of 'understanding' governs the oath of speaking.
The same principle applies across different contexts.
5
Contrast with literalist approach
Literalist schools (e.g., Zahiri) would apply the textual command without considering the 'illah, leading to different rulings in the same scenarios.
This highlights the Hanafi commitment to maqasid al-shari'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في متعلقات النصوص أي الدلالة. بالوقاع بالنص وبالأكل والشرب بدلالة النص. وعلى اعتبار هذا المعنى قيل: يدار الحكم على تلك العلة، قال الإمام القاضي أبو زيد: لو أن قوما يعدون التأفيف كرامة لا يحرم عليهم تأفيف الأبوين، وكذلك قلنا في قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِذَا نُودِي لِلصَّلَاةِ مِن يَوْمِ الْجُمُعَةِ فَاسْعَوْا إِلَىٰ ذِكْرِ اللَّهِ وَذَرُوا الْبَيْعَ﴾ [الجمعة:9]، ولو فرضنا بيعا لا يمنع العاقدين عن السعي إلى الجمعة بأن كانا في سفينة تجري إلى الجامع لا يكره البيع، وعلى هذا قلنا: إذا حلف لا يضرب امرأته فمد شعرها أو خنقها بوجه الإيلام يحنث، وإن ضربها بلا إيلام لا يحنث. وإذا حلف لا يأكل لحما فأكل لحم السمك أو الجراد لا يحنث، ولو أكل لحم الخنزير أو الإنسان يحنث لأن اللحم حقيقة ما يتولد من الدم. وإذا حلف لا يكلم فلانا فكلمه بعد موته لا يحنث لعدم الإفهام. فيقال: الحكم يدور مع العلة وجودا وعدما.
Chapter on what pertains to texts, i.e., indication. By intercourse by text and by eating and drinking by indication of the text. And upon consideration of this meaning, it is said: The ruling revolves around that cause. Imam al-Qadi Abu Zayd said: If a people consider 'uff' as an honor, it is not unlawful for them to say 'uff' to parents. Likewise we said regarding His statement: 'O you who have believed, when [the adhan] is called for the prayer on the day of Jumu'ah, then proceed to the remembrance of Allah and leave trade' [Quran 62:9]. And if we suppose a sale that does not prevent the two contractors from going to Friday prayer, such as if they were in a ship sailing to the mosque, the sale is not disliked. And upon this, we said: If one swears not to beat his wife, and he pulls her hair or strangles her with a painful manner, he breaks his oath; but if he beats her without pain, he does not break it. And if one swears not to eat meat, and he eats fish or locust meat, he does not break his oath; but if he eats pork or human flesh, he breaks it because meat [in the true sense] is what is generated from blood. And if one swears not to speak to someone, and he speaks to him after his death, he does not break his oath due to lack of understanding. So it is said: The ruling revolves with the cause in existence and non-existence.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule stating that the legal ruling (hukm) is tied to its effective cause ('illah) in both existence and non-existence. The author begins with examples from the Quran and hadith. The hadith of the Bedouin who had intercourse in Ramadan demonstrates that the kaffarah (expiation) is linked to the act of breaking the fast intentionally, not merely to the wording. Then the principle is extended: if people consider saying 'uff' as an honor, it is not prohibited for them, because the prohibition is based on causing harm (adha), which is absent. Similarly, in the Friday verse, the prohibition of selling is actually meant to prevent missing the prayer; if the sale does not hinder attending the prayer (e.g., on a ship heading to the mosque), it is not disliked. The same reasoning applies to oaths: the oath is based on the customary understanding and the effective cause. For example, if one swears not to beat his wife, and he pulls her hair painfully, he breaks the oath because the underlying cause (i.e., causing pain) is present. But if he beats her without pain (e.g., playfully), he does not break it. Regarding meat: the word 'meat' (lahm) in the oath is understood as what is generated from blood, so fish and locust (which are not blood-based) are not included, but pork and human flesh are included because they originate from blood. Similarly, speaking to a dead person does not fulfill the oath because the purpose (understanding) is absent. Thus, all rulings are tied to their causes, not the literal words.

Key Terms
العلة (al-'illah): The effective cause or ratio legis upon which the ruling is based
الحكم يدور مع العلة وجوداً وعدماً (al-hukm yaduru ma'a al-'illah wujudan wa 'adaman): The ruling exists when the cause exists and ceases when the cause ceases
التأفيف (al-ta'fif): Saying 'uff' as an expression of annoyance

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle in this passage, if a person swears not to eat meat, and then eats fish, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because fish is a type of meat.
B) He does not break his oath because fish meat is not generated from blood.
C) He breaks his oath only if he eats fish on a Friday.
D) The ruling depends on his intention at the time of the oath.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the effective cause ('illah) determines the ruling; the literal text is interpreted in light of its purpose.

Definition and Analysis of Muqtada (Required Implication)
Defining muqtada and distinguishing it from mahdhum in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define muqtada, explain its legal effect, and analyze a legal text to identify the required implication (muqtada) while distinguishing it from mahdhum.

Core Idea
Muqtada is an addition to the text that is legally necessary; it is considered as if spoken (ka-al-malfuz).

Legal texts often leave elements implied. Understanding muqtada (required implication) is essential for extracting the full ruling from a nass. This lesson dissects its definition, examines examples, and explores the Hanafi dispute on whether it differs from mahdhum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text and the implied addition
Find the explicit wording of the nass and determine what additional element must be present for the text to have legal validity. Without this addition, the ruling cannot be applied.
In 'anti taliq' (you are divorced), the word 'taliq' is a description; the implied addition is the occurrence of divorce (maṣdar).
2
Distinguish muqtada from mahdhum
Check whether the addition is necessary for legal validity (siḥḥah sharʿiyyah) – that is muqtada; or for linguistic correctness (siḥḥah lughawiyyah) – that is mahdhum. The later Hanafis differentiate, while early scholars treated them identically.
The passage follows the early view by giving a universal definition.
3
Apply the concept to the given examples
Work through the two examples: 'You are divorced' (anti taliq) and the command 'Free your slave for a thousand dirhams' responded with 'I freed'. In each, identify the muqtada and its legal effect.
In the second example, the muqtada implies a prior agency and contract of sale, making the responder liable for the payment.
4
Understand the scholarly disagreement
Contrast the early Hanafi/Shafi'i position (no distinction between muqtada and mahdhum) with the later Hanafi distinction (Fakhr al-Islam). Note how the thamarah (practical consequence) appears in the classification of examples.
The author of Usul al-Shashi sides with al-Qadi Abu Zaid, avoiding the need to differentiate.

Chapter on matters related to texts. That is, the flesh being derived from blood in existence and non-existence. The oath and its non-occurrence. It is blood-related matters, so the ruling is based on that. As for the muqtada (required implication), it is an addition to the text, i.e., that which is established by the implication of the text. A description of the addition. That is, its meaning derived from speech. The meaning of the text is only realized through it, as if the text required it for its own meaning to be valid. An example: the husband saying to his wife. The letter fa is for causation. That is, a praiseworthy attribute for her. That is, the muqtada. The legal: his statement: 'You are divorced,' for this is a description of the woman, except that the description requires the source. So the source exists by way of implication. And when he says: 'Free your slave for a thousand dirhams,' and he says: 'I freed,' the freeing occurs for the one who commanded, and he must pay the thousand. And if the one commanding intended expiation by it, it occurs for what he intended. That is because his statement: 'Free him for me' is like the verb in requiring the source, so it is as if he said: 'You are divorced with a divorce.' Know that the majority of usul scholars among our early fellow Hanafis and the followers of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on them, did not differentiate between the types of muqaddar (implicit element). But Shaykh Fakhr al-Islam and most of the later scholars, except al-Qadi Abu Zaid, differentiated, saying: 'What is established to correct the speech linguistically is the mahdhum (deleted element), and what is established to correct the speech legally is the muqtada.' So they consider 'You are divorced' and 'I divorced you' as muqtada, and 'Divorce (imperative)' as mahdhum. According to their view, it is difficult to distinguish between 'You are divorced' and 'Divorce (imperative).' The author (may Allah have mercy on him) was absolute in his definition and did not restrict the addition 'legally or rationally' when he said: 'It is an addition to the text without which the meaning of the text is not realized,' and he did not differentiate between them. Therefore, he defined it as al-Qadi defined it. According to his view, there is no need to differentiate between them. As in 'al-Fusul'.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage from 'Usul al-Shashi' with its commentary 'Ahsan al-Hawashi' defines the concept of 'muqtada' (required implication) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by placing muqtada in the context of matters related to texts (mutāllqāt al-nusūs). The commentary clarifies that muqtada is an addition to the text (ziyādah ʿalā al-nass) that is essential for the meaning of the text to be realized. Without this implied addition, the text's legal meaning would be incomplete or impossible to implement. The example given is the husband's statement 'You are divorced' (anti ṭāliq) – the word 'divorced' is a description that requires a source (maṣdar), i.e., the act of divorce. The muqtada is that the divorce has actually occurred, even though not explicitly stated. Another detailed example is the command 'Free your slave for a thousand dirhams' followed by the agent saying 'I freed.' Here, the muqtada implies a prior sale (bayʿ) and agency (wakālah) because freeing a slave not owned is invalid. The commentary also highlights a scholarly disagreement among Hanafis: the early scholars and the Shafi'is did not differentiate between muqtada and mahdhum (deleted element), while later scholars like Fakhr al-Islam distinguished based on whether the correction is linguistic (for mahdhūf) or legal (for muqtada). The author of Usul al-Shashi, al-Shashi, follows the view of al-Qadi Abu Zaid, who did not require this distinction, defining muqtada broadly as any addition necessary for the meaning of the text.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtadā): An addition to the text without which the meaning of the text is not realized; it is as if the text required it for its own validity.
المحذوف (al-mahdhūf): An implicit element deleted from the text linguistically for brevity, but the meaning is understood; it is established to correct the speech linguistically.
النص (al-nass): The explicit wording of a legal text (Qur'an, hadith, or speech) from which legal rulings are derived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The word 'divorced' itself
B) The implied source (maṣdar) of divorce, i.e., 'divorce' occurring
C) The husband's intention
D) The wife's response

Key Takeaway
Muqtada is an essential implied element for legal validity; it is treated as part of the text (ka-al-malfuz) and must be recognized to fully understand and apply Islamic legal texts.

The Principle of Iqtida': Implication by Necessity
Explaining the principle of iqtida' and its application to divorce and eating oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi concept of muqtada, apply the principle of limitation by necessity to divorce and oath examples, and explain why the implied meaning has no generality.

Core Idea
Muqtada is established only to the extent of necessity; it has no generality and cannot be specified.

In this passage from al-Shashi's Usul, we learn a foundational principle: when the validity of a statement depends on an unstated meaning, that meaning is established only to the minimum extent necessary. This principle governs how we interpret oaths, divorces, and other legal speech acts. We will explore two key examples and understand why the Hanafi school rejects specification by intention in such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The passage begins by stating that the implied meaning (muqtada) is established by way of necessity (الضرورة), and it is estimated to the extent of necessity. The key phrase is 'الضرورة فيقدر بقدر الضرورة'.
This is the core rule.
2
Apply to divorce intention
If someone says 'You are divorced' but intends three divorces, only one divorce occurs. The necessity of making the statement meaningful is met by a single divorce; the additional two are beyond necessity and do not occur.
The intention of three does not increase the number.
3
Apply to eating oath
If someone says 'If I eat' intending a specific food (e.g., bread), the oath is broken by eating any food. The implied object is muqtada, and since necessity is removed by any absolute individual (فرد مطلق), there is no room for specification.
No generality means no specification.
4
Understand the reasoning
Specification depends on generality, but the implied meaning (muqtada) has no generality. It is not a general term that can be specified; it is simply the minimum necessary for the statement to be valid.
Contrast with muqaddar (lexically implied) which may have generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في متعلقات النصوص: لثبوت المقتضى بطريق الضرورة. الضرورة فيقدر بقدر الضرورة، ولهذا قلنا: إذا قال: «أنت طالق» ونوى به الطلاق الثلاث لا يصح؛ لأن الطلاق يقدر مذكورا بطريق الاقتضاء فيقدر بقدر الضرورة والضرورة ترتفع بالواحد فيقدر مذكورا في حق الواحد. وهذا يخرج الحكم في قوله: «إن أكلت» ونوى به طعاما دون طعام لا يصح؛ لأن الأكل يقتضي طعاما فكان ذلك ثابتا بطريق الاقتضاء فيقدر بقدر الضرورة والضرورة ترتفع بالفرد المطلق ولا تخصيص في الفرد المطلق؛ لأن التخصيص يعتمد العموم، ولا عموم للمقتضي. (قال الحاشية: هذه المسألة خلافية بيننا وبين الشافعي رحمه الله، فعنده يجوز تخصيصه بالنية ديانة لا قضاء بناء على أن الأكل فعل متعد ولا بد له من مفعول ملفوظ أو مقدر فيقدر هاهنا «شيء» أو «طعام» وهو عام يجوز تخصيصه بالنية، وعند أصحابنا لا يجوز أصلا بناء على أنه ليس مقدرا بل مقتضى؛ لأن الأكل اعتبر لازما وإن كان متعديا كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن كَانَ مِنكُم مَّرِيضًا﴾ [البقرة:184] وقوله: ﴿وَلاَ يَعْلَمُونَ﴾ [البقرة:13] و﴿وَهُمْ لاَ يَشْعُرُونَ﴾ [الأعراف:95]، وغير ذلك كثير وله باب واسع في المعاني، فيقدر كأنه قال: «لا يقع مني الأكل» فتوقف الفعل المتعدي على وجود المفعول باعتبار الواقع مسلم، لكنه باعتبار التصور والفهم والإرادة غير مسلم في المنزل منزلة اللازم، فإذا لم يكن الطعام مقصودا ومرادا من اللفظ ولا مفهوما منه لزوما، بل مما يتوقف عليه وجودا لم يكن من قبيل عام مقدر حتى يجوز تخصيصه، فافهم، كذا في "الحصول".) وعلى هذا: لو قال: «اعتدي» ونوى به الطلاق بعد الدخول يقع الطلاق اقتضاء؛ لأن الاعتداد يقتضي وجود الطلاق فيقدر الطلاق موجودا ضرورة، ولهذا كان الواقع به رجعيا؛ لأن صفة البينونة زائدة على قدر الضرورة فلا يثبت بطريق الاقتضاء ولا يقع إلا واحد.
Chapter on the implications of texts: For the establishment of the implied meaning by way of necessity. Necessity is estimated to the extent of necessity. For this we said: If a man says 'You are divorced' and intends by it three divorces, it is not valid; because divorce is estimated as mentioned by way of iqtida', so it is estimated to the extent of necessity, and necessity is removed by one, so it is estimated as mentioned with respect to one. And this brings out the ruling in his statement: 'If I eat' and he intends by it a specific food [not another], it is not valid; because eating implies food, so that is established by way of iqtida', so it is estimated to the extent of necessity, and necessity is removed by the absolute individual, and there is no specification in the absolute individual; because specification depends on generality, and the implied meaning has no generality. (The footnote says: This issue is disputed between us and al-Shafi'i - may Allah have mercy on him. According to him, it is permissible to specify it by intention as a matter of conscience but not in judicial proceedings, based on the fact that 'eating' is a transitive verb and must have an object expressed or implied, so here 'something' or 'food' is implied, which is general and it is permissible to specify it by intention. According to our scholars, it is not permissible at all, based on the fact that it is not implied but rather an entailment; because 'eating' is considered intransitive even though it is transitive, as in His statement: 'So whoever among you is ill' [al-Baqarah:184] and His statement: 'And they do not know' [al-Baqarah:13] and 'And they are unaware' [al-A'raf:95], and many others, and it has a wide scope in meanings. So it is estimated as if he said: 'Eating will not occur from me.' The dependency of the transitive verb on the existence of the object is conceded in terms of actual occurrence, but in terms of conception, understanding, and intention, it is not conceded for what is treated as intransitive. So when the food is not intended or meant by the wording, nor understood from it necessarily, but rather is something on which existence depends, it is not of the category of an implied general term that can be specified. So understand, as in 'al-Husul'.) And upon this: If he says 'Observe the waiting period' and intends divorce after consummation, divorce occurs by way of iqtida'; because observing the waiting period implies the existence of divorce, so divorce is estimated as existing necessarily, and for this reason the divorce that occurs is revocable; because the characteristic of irrevocability is additional to the extent of necessity, so it is not established by way of iqtida', and only one divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage from al-Shashi's Usul discusses the principle of iqtida' (implication by necessity). The core idea is that when the validity of a statement depends on an unstated meaning, that meaning is established only to the minimum extent needed. The author provides three examples: (1) If someone says 'You are divorced' but intends three divorces, only one divorce occurs because the necessity of the statement (making it meaningful) is fulfilled by a single divorce. (2) If someone says 'If I eat' but intends a specific food (e.g., bread not watermelon), the oath is broken by eating any food because the implied object is not subject to specification; necessity requires only any food. (3) If a husband tells his wife 'Observe the waiting period' after consummation and intends divorce, a revocable divorce occurs because the necessity of the command (making it meaningful) requires only the existence of divorce, not irrevocability. The hashiya then clarifies a dispute with al-Shafi'i. Al-Shafi'i allows specifying the implied food by intention (in conscience but not in court) because he considers the object as an implied general term. The Hanafis reject this because the object is an entailment (muqtada), not a lexical implication; the verb 'eat' is treated as intransitive in this context, as seen in Quranic usage. The entailment has no generality, so it cannot be specified. The passage emphasizes that the implied meaning is limited to the extent of necessity, and any addition is invalid.

Key Terms
مقتضى (muqtada): المعنى اللازم للفظ الذي لا يصح الكلام بدونه، ويثبت بقدر الضرورة
ضرورة (darura): الحاجة التي ترفع بها صحة الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three
B) One
C) None
D) Two

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the implied meaning (muqtada) is established only to the minimum extent necessary for speech to be valid; it has no generality and cannot be specified by intention.

The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Specifying Muqtada by Intention
Clarifying the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on specifying muqtada and its application to the waiting period example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on specifying the implied object by intention, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) for oaths.

Core Idea
Hanafis hold that muqtada cannot be specified by intention; Shafi'is allow it in conscience but not in court.

The hashiya attached to al-Shashi's text addresses a key disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. At issue is whether a person who swears 'If I eat' can limit the oath to a specific food by his intention. The answer depends on how we view the verb 'eat'—is it transitive with a general implied object, or is it treated as intransitive with a non-general entailment? This lesson dissects both proofs and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
Al-Shafi'i allows the muqtada (implied food) to be specified by intention as a matter of conscience (diyanatan) but not in court. Hanafis disallow it absolutely.
2
Examine the Shafi'i proof
Shafi'i argues that 'eating' is a transitive verb, so it must have an object. The object is implied as 'something' or 'food', which is a general term. Since it is general, it can be specified by intention.
This is based on the principle that general terms can be specified by intention in din.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
Hanafis argue that 'eating' is treated as intransitive in this context, citing Quranic examples like 'فمن كان منكم مريضا' where 'كان' is used without a predicate. They say the implied object is not a lexical implication but a logical entailment (muqtada), which has no generality.
The key is the distinction between muqtada and muqaddar.
4
Understand the nuance
The dependency of the transitive verb on the object is conceded in terms of actual existence (wujud), but not in terms of conception and intention. Since the eater does not have a specific food in mind, the implied object is not meant or understood from the wording.
Thus, it is not a general term that can be specified.
5
Apply to the waiting period example
If a husband says 'Observe the waiting period' intending divorce, a revocable divorce occurs. The irrevocability is beyond necessity, so it is not established by iqtida'.
This illustrates that only the minimum necessary is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في متعلقات النصوص: لثبوت المقتضى بطريق الضرورة. الضرورة فيقدر بقدر الضرورة، ولهذا قلنا: إذا قال: «أنت طالق» ونوى به الطلاق الثلاث لا يصح؛ لأن الطلاق يقدر مذكورا بطريق الاقتضاء فيقدر بقدر الضرورة والضرورة ترتفع بالواحد فيقدر مذكورا في حق الواحد. وهذا يخرج الحكم في قوله: «إن أكلت» ونوى به طعاما دون طعام لا يصح؛ لأن الأكل يقتضي طعاما فكان ذلك ثابتا بطريق الاقتضاء فيقدر بقدر الضرورة والضرورة ترتفع بالفرد المطلق ولا تخصيص في الفرد المطلق؛ لأن التخصيص يعتمد العموم، ولا عموم للمقتضي. (قال الحاشية: هذه المسألة خلافية بيننا وبين الشافعي رحمه الله، فعنده يجوز تخصيصه بالنية ديانة لا قضاء بناء على أن الأكل فعل متعد ولا بد له من مفعول ملفوظ أو مقدر فيقدر هاهنا «شيء» أو «طعام» وهو عام يجوز تخصيصه بالنية، وعند أصحابنا لا يجوز أصلا بناء على أنه ليس مقدرا بل مقتضى؛ لأن الأكل اعتبر لازما وإن كان متعديا كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن كَانَ مِنكُم مَّرِيضًا﴾ [البقرة:184] وقوله: ﴿وَلاَ يَعْلَمُونَ﴾ [البقرة:13] و﴿وَهُمْ لاَ يَشْعُرُونَ﴾ [الأعراف:95]، وغير ذلك كثير وله باب واسع في المعاني، فيقدر كأنه قال: «لا يقع مني الأكل» فتوقف الفعل المتعدي على وجود المفعول باعتبار الواقع مسلم، لكنه باعتبار التصور والفهم والإرادة غير مسلم في المنزل منزلة اللازم، فإذا لم يكن الطعام مقصودا ومرادا من اللفظ ولا مفهوما منه لزوما، بل مما يتوقف عليه وجودا لم يكن من قبيل عام مقدر حتى يجوز تخصيصه، فافهم، كذا في "الحصول".) وعلى هذا: لو قال: «اعتدي» ونوى به الطلاق بعد الدخول يقع الطلاق اقتضاء؛ لأن الاعتداد يقتضي وجود الطلاق فيقدر الطلاق موجودا ضرورة، ولهذا كان الواقع به رجعيا؛ لأن صفة البينونة زائدة على قدر الضرورة فلا يثبت بطريق الاقتضاء ولا يقع إلا واحد.
Chapter on the implications of texts: For the establishment of the implied meaning by way of necessity. Necessity is estimated to the extent of necessity. For this we said: If a man says 'You are divorced' and intends by it three divorces, it is not valid; because divorce is estimated as mentioned by way of iqtida', so it is estimated to the extent of necessity, and necessity is removed by one, so it is estimated as mentioned with respect to one. And this brings out the ruling in his statement: 'If I eat' and he intends by it a specific food [not another], it is not valid; because eating implies food, so that is established by way of iqtida', so it is estimated to the extent of necessity, and necessity is removed by the absolute individual, and there is no specification in the absolute individual; because specification depends on generality, and the implied meaning has no generality. (The footnote says: This issue is disputed between us and al-Shafi'i - may Allah have mercy on him. According to him, it is permissible to specify it by intention as a matter of conscience but not in judicial proceedings, based on the fact that 'eating' is a transitive verb and must have an object expressed or implied, so here 'something' or 'food' is implied, which is general and it is permissible to specify it by intention. According to our scholars, it is not permissible at all, based on the fact that it is not implied but rather an entailment; because 'eating' is considered intransitive even though it is transitive, as in His statement: 'So whoever among you is ill' [al-Baqarah:184] and His statement: 'And they do not know' [al-Baqarah:13] and 'And they are unaware' [al-A'raf:95], and many others, and it has a wide scope in meanings. So it is estimated as if he said: 'Eating will not occur from me.' The dependency of the transitive verb on the existence of the object is conceded in terms of actual occurrence, but in terms of conception, understanding, and intention, it is not conceded for what is treated as intransitive. So when the food is not intended or meant by the wording, nor understood from it necessarily, but rather is something on which existence depends, it is not of the category of an implied general term that can be specified. So understand, as in 'al-Husul'.) And upon this: If he says 'Observe the waiting period' and intends divorce after consummation, divorce occurs by way of iqtida'; because observing the waiting period implies the existence of divorce, so divorce is estimated as existing necessarily, and for this reason the divorce that occurs is revocable; because the characteristic of irrevocability is additional to the extent of necessity, so it is not established by way of iqtida', and only one divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage from al-Shashi's Usul discusses the principle of iqtida' (implication by necessity). The core idea is that when the validity of a statement depends on an unstated meaning, that meaning is established only to the minimum extent needed. The author provides three examples: (1) If someone says 'You are divorced' but intends three divorces, only one divorce occurs because the necessity of the statement (making it meaningful) is fulfilled by a single divorce. (2) If someone says 'If I eat' but intends a specific food (e.g., bread not watermelon), the oath is broken by eating any food because the implied object is not subject to specification; necessity requires only any food. (3) If a husband tells his wife 'Observe the waiting period' after consummation and intends divorce, a revocable divorce occurs because the necessity of the command (making it meaningful) requires only the existence of divorce, not irrevocability. The hashiya then clarifies a dispute with al-Shafi'i. Al-Shafi'i allows specifying the implied food by intention (in conscience but not in court) because he considers the object as an implied general term. The Hanafis reject this because the object is an entailment (muqtada), not a lexical implication; the verb 'eat' is treated as intransitive in this context, as seen in Quranic usage. The entailment has no generality, so it cannot be specified. The passage emphasizes that the implied meaning is limited to the extent of necessity, and any addition is invalid.

Key Terms
مقتضى (muqtada): المعنى اللازم للفظ الذي لا يصح الكلام بدونه، ويثبت بقدر الضرورة
ضرورة (darura): الحاجة التي ترفع بها صحة الكلام
تعدي (ta'addi): كون الفعل متعديا أي يحتاج إلى مفعول به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, why can the implied food in 'If I eat' not be specified by intention?
A) Because the verb is transitive and the object is general.
B) Because the verb is treated as intransitive and the implied object is muqtada with no generality.
C) Because intention is not considered in oaths at all.
D) Because the Quran prohibits such specification.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that muqtada (logical entailment) has no generality and cannot be specified by intention, unlike muqaddar (lexical implication) which may be general.
