Definition of Al-Amr: The Hanafi View on Command and Obligation
Defining al-amr and refuting the restriction to imperative form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of al-amr (command), distinguish its linguistic and technical meanings, and explain why the imperative formula is not the exclusive bearer of obligation.

Core Idea
Al-Amr is not merely the imperative form; it is the imposition of an action with superiority, and obligation can be established without it.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi defines 'al-amr' (command) as a foundational concept in Hanafi legal theory. The author provides the linguistic and technical definitions, then addresses a crucial objection: that al-amr is restricted to the specific Arabic imperative formula (ifʿal). Through reasoning rooted in the eternal nature of Allah's speech and the obligation of faith upon those without revelation, the Hanafi school demonstrates that obligation (wujūb) is broader than the verbal form. This lesson will guide you through the definition, the objection, and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Read the passage and extract both the linguistic and technical (sharʿī) definitions of al-amr. Note the key phrase: 'qawl al-qāʼil li-ghayrihi ifʿal ʿalā sabīl al-istiʿlāʼ'.
The same definition is given in both contexts, but the technical usage carries the legal effect of obligation.
2
Isolate the contested claim
Identify the claim attributed to 'some imams' that al-amr is specific to the imperative formula (ifʿal). Understand that this would restrict command to a particular verbal form.
3
Analyze the first objection
Follow the author's first objection: if al-amr were solely the verbal formula, it could not be attributed to Allah in pre-eternity, since the formula is created. This conflicts with the Ahl al-Sunnah belief that Allah's speech is eternal.
This objection uses the attribute of divine speech (kalām) to refute the restriction.
4
Analyze the second objection
Consider the second objection: obligation (wujūb) is established even without the imperative formula. The example given is the obligation of faith (īmān) upon those who never received revelation. Cite Abu Hanifa's statement that rational people would be obligated to know Allah by their intellects.
This shows that the intended meaning (murād) of the Lawgiver—obligation—is not tied to a specific verbal form.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
Synthesize the points: al-amr in its true sense is the imposition of an action with superiority, not merely a verbal formula. The servant's duty in sharʿī matters is determined by the command, but the underlying obligation can arise from other sources like reason.
This conclusion affirms that al-amr is defined by its effect (obligation) and condition (istiʿlāʼ), not restricted to the imperative form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأمر
الأمر في اللغة قول القائل لغيره افعل على سبيل الاستعلاء. وفي الشرع قول القائل لغيره افعل على سبيل الاستعلاء. وهو الوجوب: إلزام الفعل على الغير. وذكر بعض الأئمة أن المراد بالأمر يختص بهذه الصيغة أي افعل. واستحال أن يكون معناه أن حقيقة الأمر يختص بهذه الصيغة فإن اعتراض أول. خلافا للمعتزلة فإن كلامه تعالى عندهم ألفاظ حادثة. الله تعالى متكلم في الأزل عندنا وكلامه أمر ونهي وإخبار واستخبار، أي عند أهل السنة والجماعة. فكيف قالوا إن المراد بالأمر يختص بهذه الصيغة. واستحال وجود هذه الصيغة في الأزل واستحال أيضا أن يكون معناه أن المراد بالأمر للآمر يختص بهذه الصيغة، فإن المراد للشارع بالأمر وجوب الفعل على العبد وهو معنى الابتلاء عندنا وقد ثبت الوجوب بدون هذه الصيغة أ ليس أنه وجب الإيمان على من لم تبلغه الدعوة بدون ورود السمع، قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: لو لم يبعث الله تعالى رسولا لوجب على العقلاء معرفته بعقولهم، فيحمل ذلك على أن المراد بالأمر يختص بهذه الصيغة في قول البعض دون الشارع. حق العبد في الشرعيات
A section on Al-Amr (the command). Al-Amr linguistically is the statement of a speaker to another saying 'do' in a manner of superiority. And in the Shari'ah it is the statement of a speaker to another saying 'do' in a manner of superiority. And it is obligation (wujub): imposing the doing of an action upon another. And some of the imams mentioned that the meaning of al-amr is specific to this formula, i.e., 'do'. And it is impossible that its meaning is that the reality of al-amr is specific to this formula, as a first objection. Contrary to the Mu'tazila, for the speech of Allah Most High in their view is created expressions. Allah Most High is a speaker in pre-eternity according to us, and His speech is command, prohibition, information, and interrogation, i.e., according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah. So how can they say that the meaning of al-amr is specific to this formula? And its existence in pre-eternity is impossible. And it is also impossible that its meaning is that what is intended by the command of the commander is specific to this formula. For what is intended by the Lawgiver by the command is the obligation of the action upon the servant, and that is the meaning of trial (ibtilā’) according to us. And obligation has been established without this formula. Is it not that faith became obligatory upon the one whom the call did not reach without the arrival of revelation? Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'If Allah had not sent a messenger, it would have been obligatory upon the rational people to know Him by their intellects.' So this is interpreted as the meaning of al-amr being specific to this formula in the view of some, not the Lawgiver. The right of the servant in the matters of Sharīʿah.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of the term 'al-amr' (command) in both linguistic and technical usage, as treated in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by giving the linguistic definition: the statement of a speaker to another saying 'do' (if‘al) in a manner of superiority (isti‘lā’). The technical (shar‘ī) definition is the same, but with the added effect that it implies obligation (wujūb), which is the imposition of an action upon another. The author then addresses a view held by some imams that the reality of al-amr is restricted to the specific Arabic imperative form 'if‘al'. He raises two objections to this view. First, if al-amr were solely the verbal formula, then it would be impossible to attribute command to Allah in pre-eternity (azal) because the verbal formula is created and temporally originated (hādith). But according to Ahl al-Sunnah, Allah's speech (kalām) is eternal and encompasses command, prohibition, etc. Therefore, the command cannot be limited to the formula. Second, the intended meaning (murād) of the Lawgiver by command is the obligation of the action upon the servant, which is also called 'ibtilā’' (trial). Obligation is established even without the formula, as seen in the case of people who did not receive prophetic call (da‘wah) yet faith (īmān) is obligatory upon them through intellectual reasoning. The author cites Abu Hanifa's well-known position that if no messenger were sent, rational adults would still be obligated to know Allah through their intellect. Thus, the idea that al-amr is specific to the formula is rejected when applied to the Lawgiver; rather, it may be valid when used by others. The passage concludes by noting that the servant's duty (haqq al-‘abd) in shar‘ī matters is determined by the command formula, but the underlying obligation is broader.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): قول القائل لغيره افعل على سبيل الاستعلاء، وهو في الشرع موجب للوجوب
الوجوب (al-wujūb): إلزام الفعل على الغير، وهو حكم الأمر عند الإطلاق
الاستعلاء (al-istiʿlāʾ): جعل نفسه أعلى من المخاطب في الأمر، فيخرج به الاستفهام والتمني ونحوهما

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of al-amr in the Shari'ah?
A) A request for an action without superiority
B) The statement 'do' (ifʿal) only
C) The imposition of an action upon another in a manner of superiority, implying obligation
D) Any speech that seeks an action

Key Takeaway
Al-amr is a command with superiority and implies obligation; it is not confined to the imperative form but includes any means that establishes obligation.

Definition of al-Amr and Obligation Without the Imperative Form
Definition of al-amr and proof of obligation without the imperative form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-amr in Hanafi usul, explain why obligation can be established without the imperative form, and apply Abu Hanifa's rational proof to identify obligation in non-imperative texts.

Core Idea
Obligation can exist without the imperative form: rational necessity alone can prove wujub.

This passage defines al-amr (command) and its relationship to the imperative form (sighah). The base text states that al-amr is specific to the imperative form for the commander. But the commentator immediately expands: the Lawgiver's command implies obligation, and that obligation can be proven without the imperative form. Abu Hanifa's rational proof—that even without a messenger, rational beings must know Allah—demonstrates this principle. The passage also reconciles this with the Quranic verse 'We do not punish until We send a messenger.' Let's unpack the layers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its limits
The passage begins: 'The intended meaning of command for the commander is specific to this form.' First, identify what 'this form' refers to (صيغة افعل). Note that the definition is from the perspective of the commander (الآمر). Then the Lawgiver's intent is distinguished: 'the intended meaning of the Lawgiver by command is the obligation of the act upon the servant.'
The definition of al-amr is tied to the imperative form for the human commander, but for the Lawgiver the essence is obligation.
2
Recognize the expansion: obligation without the form
The matn says 'obligation has been established without this form.' The commentary provides examples: 'kutiba alaykum al-siyam' (Quran) and 'al-jihad madi' (hadith). The imperative form does not appear, yet obligation is clear. Also, rational obligation (knowing Allah without revelation) is cited.
The expansion (شرح) broadens the concept: the imperative form is not the exclusive proof of obligation.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's rational proof
Abu Hanifa states: 'If Allah had not sent a messenger, it would be obligatory upon the intelligent to know Him by their intellects.' This is a dalil aqli (rational proof). The wajh al-dalalah: if obligation required the imperative form, those without revelation would have no duty; but they do have a duty (knowing Allah), so obligation can exist without the form.
The proof is qiyasi in nature, establishing a universal principle: obligation stems from the Lawgiver's will, not merely the verbal form.
4
Reconcile with the Quranic verse
An objection is raised: the Quran says 'We do not punish until We send a messenger.' The response: the verse applies to revealed laws after belief in Allah. The primordial obligation to know Allah is different – it does not depend on revelation. The hashiya adds that the period of experimentation (مدة التجربة) is needed, like a call.
This shows careful reconciliation: the rational obligation is pre-revelation, while punishment for not following revealed laws occurs only after a messenger.
5
Apply the distinction to shari'ah matters
The passage concludes: in shari'ah matters, the obligation upon us appears through the imperative form. But the obligation from Allah in eternity does not depend on it. This means when we encounter a non-imperative text that clearly indicates obligation (e.g., 'kutiba'), we classify it as wajib.
The distinction between the Lawgiver's eternal command and the form we receive is crucial for istinbat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأمر
المراد بالأمر للآمر يختص بهذه الصيغة، فإن المراد للشارع بالأمر وجوب الفعل على العبد وهو معنى الابتلاء عندنا وقد ثبت الوجوب بدون هذه الصيغة.
قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: لو لم يبعث الله تعالى رسولا لوجب على العقلاء معرفته بعقولهم، فيحمل ذلك على أن المراد بالأمر يختص بهذه الصيغة في حق العبد في الشرعيات دون الشارع.
(قوله: [وهو معنى الابتلاء عندنا] هذه الجملة معترضة أي وجوب الفعل، وهو المراد بالابتلاء عندنا يعني: أنّ الله تعالى ابتلى العبد بوجوب الفعل عليه، إن فعل أثاب وإن ترك عاقب، كذا في "المعدن".
قوله [ بدون ورود السمع بل ثبت الوجوب في الفروع الشرعية الموقوفة على الشرع أيضاً بدون هذه الصيغة، كما في قوله تعالى: {كتب عليكم الصيام} [البقرة: 183]، وقوله: {كتب عليكم القتال} [البقرة: 216]، وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «الجهاد ماض» وقول الشارع: «وجب أو فرض عليكم» ولو أدخلت هذه الألفاظ في الأمر حكماً فليدخل الأمر العقلي والفعلي فيه أيضاً حكماً، فافهم، فقد سقط ما أوّله المصنف رحمه الله.
قوله: [قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله ... إلخ] فإن قيل قول أبي حنيفة: «لو لم يبعث... إلخ» مخالف للنص وهو قوله تعالى: {وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا} [الإسراء: 15]، قيل في جوابه: النص محمول على الشرائع التي يفترض على المكلف بعد الإيمان بالله تعالى، وإن كان الإيجاب من تعالى في الأزل لا يتوقف على صيغة الأمر.
قوله: [معرفته بعقولهم] المراد بالمعرفة الإيمان بالله تعالى، لكن ذكر المعرفة؛ لأنها سبب الإيمان حتى لو لم يعرفوه ولم يأمنوا كانوا معذورين، فثبت أنّ الإيمان يجب بدون هذه الصيغة، وهذا محمول فيما أدرك زمان مدة التجربة والمهلة لدرك العواقب؛ لأنّ من مات قبل ذلك بعد البلوغ و لم يعتقد إيماناً ولا كفراً يكون معذوراً؛ لأنّ إدراك زمان مدّة التجربة بمنزلة الدعوة، فلا بد منه عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله.
قوله: [في الشرعيّات ... إلخ] يعني أنّ الوجوب علينا في التكليفات التي وجبت بالشرع فقط غير وجبت بالعقل كالإيمان بالله تعالى وصفاته لا يظهر لنا إلا بصيغة الأمر، وإن كان الإيجاب من الله تعالى في الأزل لا يتوقف على صيغة الأمر.)
Section on Command. The intended meaning of command for the commander is specific to this form. Indeed, the intended meaning of the Lawgiver by command is the obligation of the act upon the servant, and that is the meaning of trial according to us, and obligation has been established without this form. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: If Allah Almighty had not sent a messenger, it would be obligatory upon the intelligent to know Him by their intellects. So that is interpreted to mean that the intended meaning of command is specific to this form with respect to the servant in legal matters, not the Lawgiver.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an usul definition expansion. It defines al-amr (command) and its specific association with the imperative form (sighah). The base text (matn) states that command is specific to the form 'if'al', but the commentator (sharh) immediately clarifies that obligation (wujub) can be established without this form, citing the obligation of faith for those not reached by revelation (Abu Hanifa's rational proof). The hashiya (footnotes) elaborate: the obligation of fasting and fighting is proven by texts not using the imperative, like 'kutiba alaykum' (written/obliged). It also reconciles Abu Hanifa's view with the Quranic verse that punishment only occurs after sending a messenger, explaining that the verse applies to revealed laws after belief in Allah, but the primordial obligation from Allah does not depend on the imperative form. The passage concludes that in shari'ah matters, obligation appears through the imperative form, though Allah's command in eternity does not rely on it. This is a typical Hanafi usul position: amm is qat'i in the absence of takhsis, and obligation can be known by reason (dalil aqli).

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): A statement that demands an action from a superior to an inferior, specifically via the imperative form (صيغة افعل)
الوجوب (al-wujub): A legal obligation that necessitates performance, with reward for doing and punishment for leaving

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is specific to the imperative form 'if'al'.
B) It is the obligation of the act upon the servant.
C) It is only proven by the imperative form.
D) It is a recommendation, not an obligation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, obligation (wujub) can be established without the imperative form—by non-imperative textual expressions and by rational necessity.

The Absolute Command Implies Obligation
States Hanafi position on absolute command with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the absolute command, apply the proof from disobedience/obedience, and evaluate the opposing view of tawaqquf.

Core Idea
Absolute command is for obligation until evidence says otherwise.

This lesson delves into a foundational usul dispute: when the Lawgiver issues a command without any contextual clue, what is its default legal meaning? The Hanafi school holds firmly that it signifies obligation (wujūb). We will unpack their proof and contrast it with the opposing view that the matter should be suspended until clarification.

Guided Path
1
Define the absolute command
Identify what is meant by 'al-amr al-mutlaq' – a command stripped of any qarīnah (contextual evidence) about its legal force.
The text gives examples: Quran 7:204 (listen when Quran is recited) and 2:35 (do not approach the tree).
2
State the Hanafi position
The correct position in the madhhab is that its implication is obligation (wujūb) unless a contrary evidence appears.
'al-ṣaḥīḥ min al-madhhab' – the correct view of the school.
3
Analyze the proof
The proof: neglecting a command is disobedience (ma'siyah) and obeying is obedience (ṭā'ah). This binary only makes sense if the command is for obligation. If it were for recommendation, neglect would not be sinful.
The poetic line from al-Ḥamāsī reinforces that obeying a commander is taken as required.
4
Contrast the opposing view
Ibn Surayj and al-Ghazālī (Shafi'i) argued that the word 'amr' is used for multiple meanings, so the default should be tawaqquf (suspension) until context clarifies.
The Hanafi response: multiplicity does not prevent haqīqah in wujūb; other meanings are majāz needing evidence.
5
Extract the practical consequence
In practice, if a ruler says 'Do this', Hanafis deem it obligatory; the opposing view treats it as a mere request until proven otherwise.
This thamarah al-khilaf shows real impact on ijtihad and fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل اختلف الناس في الأمر المطلق أي المجرد عن القرينة الدالة على اللزوم وعدم اللزوم، نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا قُرِئَ الْقُرْآنُ فَاسْتَمِعُوا لَهُ وَأَنصِتُوا لَعَلَّكُمْ تُرْحَمُونَ﴾ [الأعراف: ٢٠٤] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْرَبَا هَذه الشَّجَرَةَ فَتَكُونَا منَ الظَّالمينَ﴾ [البقرة: ٣٥]، والصحيح من المذهب أن موجبه الوجوب إلا إذا قام الدليل على خلافه؛ لأن ترك الأمر معصية كما أن الايتمار طاعة، قال الحماسي: أَطَعْتِ لَآمِرِيْكَ بِصَرْم حَبْلِي مُرِيهُمْ فِي أَحبَتِهِمْ بِذَاكَ
Chapter: The people differed regarding the absolute command, i.e., stripped of any contextual evidence indicating obligation or non-obligation, like His statement, 'And when the Quran is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy' [7:204], and His statement, 'And do not approach this tree, for then you would be among the wrongdoers' [2:35]. The correct position in the madhhab is that its implication is obligation unless evidence to the contrary exists, because neglecting a command is disobedience just as obeying it is obedience. Al-Hamasī said: 'You obeyed your commander in cutting my rope; command them in their beloved ones with that.'
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the central Hanafi position on the absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq). The issue: when a command is given without any qarīnah (contextual evidence) to specify whether it is for obligation (wujūb), recommendation (nadb), or mere permission (ibāḥah), what is its default legal implication? The Hanafi madhhab (the correct position) holds that it implies obligation. This is proven by the fact that neglecting a command is considered ma'siyah (disobedience) and obeying it is ṭā'ah (obedience). The poetic quote from al-Hamasī illustrates that obeying a commander is natural; if there were no obligation, neglecting it would not be sinful. The contrary positions of Ibn Surayj (tawaqquf) and al-Ghazālī (tawaqquf in meaning) are rejected. The Hanafi view is that the absolute command is for wujūb until evidence diverts it to another meaning.

Key Terms
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-mutlaq): A command devoid of any contextual evidence (qarīnah) indicating the specific legal implication (e.g., obligation, recommendation).
الايتمار (al-imtithāl): Obeying the command; performing the commanded action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the default implication of an absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq)?
A) It implies recommendation (nadb)
B) It implies obligation (wujūb) unless evidence to the contrary
C) It implies permissibility (ibāḥah)
D) Its meaning is suspended (tawaqquf) until clarified

Key Takeaway
The absolute command in Hanafi usul defaults to obligation; only a clear contextual clue can divert it to another meaning.

The Absolute Command: Obligation or Suspension?
Dispute over whether absolute command indicates obligation or requires pause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the absolute command and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the concept of customary disobedience (ma‘ṣiya).

Core Idea
An absolute command implies obligation because violating it is called disobedience, which deserves punishment.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces a foundational dispute: when a command appears in the Quran or Sunnah without any qualifying evidence, what is its legal implication? The Hanafi school holds that it indicates obligation (wujub), while some Shafi'is, like Ibn al-Sharih, argue for suspension (tawaqquf) until the intended meaning is clarified. The Hanafi proof is rooted in custom ('urf): leaving a command is termed 'disobedience', and disobedience warrants punishment, so the command must be obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
What is the exact point of disagreement? The implication of an absolute command (amr mutlaq), i.e., a command form free from any evidence indicating whether it requires obligation or not.
The issue is not the linguistic meaning of 'amr' but its legal effect in Shari'ah texts.
2
State the Hanafi position
The correct view from the Hanafi madhhab is that the absolute command necessitates obligation (wujub) unless evidence to the contrary appears.
This is the mufta bihi position; the text explicitly says 'al-ṣaḥīḥ min al-madhhab'.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The proof is that leaving a command is called disobedience (ma‘ṣiya) both in custom and in Shari'ah, and disobedience deserves punishment. Therefore, the command must be obligatory, because punishment is only for neglecting an obligation.
The text uses a line of poetry to illustrate that obeying a valid command is called obedience and violating it is called disobedience.
4
Analyze the opposing position (Ibn al-Sharih)
Ibn al-Sharih (a Shafi'i) argues that the command form is used for many meanings (recommendation, reprimand, challenge, etc.), so it is homonymous. Probability (ihtimal) necessitates suspension (tawaqquf) until context clarifies.
He provides examples like 'then write for them' (recommendation) and 'do whatever you will' (reprimand).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response and the thamarah al-khilaf
The Hanafi response is that while the command has multiple uses, the literal default (when absolute) is obligation, supported by the customary fact that violation is disobedience deserving punishment. The other uses are metaphorical and require evidence to override the default. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that when a command appears without context, the Hanafi rules it obligatory, while Ibn al-Sharih's follower pauses and does not rule until context is provided.
This disagreement affects the derivation of rulings across all fiqh chapters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في اختلاف الناس في الأمر المطلق أي علماء الأمة من المجتهدين. أي موجبه. أي المخلي. فصل اختلف الناس في الأمر المطلق أي المجرد عن القرينة الدالة على اللزوم وعدم اللزوم، نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا قُرِئَ الْقُرْآنُ فَاسْتَمِعُوا لَهُ وَأَنصِتُواْ لَعَلَّكُمْ تُرْحَمُونَ﴾ [الأعراف: ٢٠٤] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْرَبَا هَذِهِ الشَّجَرَةَ فَتَكُونَا منَ الظَّالمينَ﴾ [البقرة: ٣٥]، والصحيح من المذهب أن موجبه الوجوب إلا إذا قام الدليل على خلافه؛ لأن ترك الأمر معصية كما أن الايتمار طاعة، قال الحماسي:
أَطَعْتِ لَآمِرِيْكَ بِصَرْم حَبْلِي مُرِيهُمْ فِي أَحبَتِهِمْ بِذَاكَ
فهمْ إِن طَاوَعُوك فطاوعيهم وإن عاصوك فاعْصي مَن عَصَاكَ
والعصيان فيما يرجع إلى حق الشرع سبب للعقاب، وتحقيقه أن لزوم الايتمار إنما يكون بقدر ولاية الأمر على المخاطب ولهذا إذا وجهت صيغة الأمر إلى من لا يلزمه طاعتك أصلا لا يكون ذلك موجبا للايتمار وإذا وجهتها إلى من يلزمه طاعتك من العبيد لزمه الايتمار لا محالة حتى لو تركه اختيارا يستحق العقاب عرفا وشرعا، فعلى هذا عرفنا أن لزوم الايتمار بقدر ولاية الآمر.
(1) قوله: [اختلف الناس... إلخ فذهب ابن الشريح من أصحاب الشافعي إلى أنّ موجبه التوقف لأنه يستعمل في معان كثيرة بعضها حقيقة اتفاقاً، فعند الإطلاق يكون محتملاً لمعان كثيرة، والاحتمال يوجب التوقف إلى أن يبين المراد، وهي الندب كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَكَاتِبُوهُمْ﴾ [النور: ٣٣]، والتوبيخ كقوله تعالى: ﴿اعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ﴾ [فصلت: ٤٠]، والتعجيز كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَأْتُواْ بِسُورَةٍ مِّن مِّثْلِهِ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٣]، والإرشاد: ﴿وَاسْتَشْهِدُوا﴾ [البقرة: ٢٨٢] والامتنان نحو: ﴿كُلُواْ مِمَّا رَزَقَكُمُ اللهُ﴾ [المائدة: ٨٨]، والإكرام نحو: ﴿وادْخُلُوهَا بِسَلَام﴾ [الحجر: ٤٦]، والتأديب نحو: «كُل ممَّا يَليكَ»، والتسخير نحو: ﴿كُونُواْ قِرَدَةً﴾، والإهانة نحو: ﴿ذُق إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْعَزِيزُ الْكَريمُ﴾، والدعاء نحو: ﴿رَبَّنَا اغْفِرْ لِي﴾، والاحتقار نحو: ﴿أَلْقُوا مَا أَنتُم مُّلْقُونَ﴾ [الشعراء: ٤٣] وغير ذلك، ثُمّ اعلم التوقف عنده في تعيين المراد عند الاستعمال لا في تعيين الموضوع له؛ لأنه عنده موضوع بالاشتراك اللفظي للوجوب والندب والإباحة والتهديد، وذهب الغزالي وجماعة من المحققين إلى التوقف في تعيين الموضوع له أنه الوجوب فقط أو الندب فقط أو مشترك بينهما لفظاً، وعامة العلماء على أنه خاص للمعنى المخصوص، فقال بعضهم: إنه للندب؛ لأنه موضوع لطلب الفعل، وأدنى ما يترجح به جانب الوجود هو الندب، والصحيح من القول أنه للوجوب، كما هو المذكور في المتن هذا ملخص كتب الأصول.
(2) قوله: [طاوعوك] أي: ايتمروك، ويسمّى الايتمار طاعةً أي: إن طاوعوك في قطع محبتهم عن أحبت فطاوعيهم أي: ايتمري آمريك في صرم حبلى.
(3) قوله: [فاعصى] أي: اتركي أمر من ترك أمرك، ويسمّى ترك الأمر معصية.
(4) قوله: [وتحقيقه... إلخ أي تحقيق أن مقتضى مطلق الأمر مع عزل اللحظ عن مقتضيات القرائن الصارفة وخصوصيات المواد هو الوجوب، وأنّ مخالفة الأمر لَمّا سمّيت في العرف معصيةً كان مقتضى الأمر وموجبه الوجوب؛ لأنّ معصية الله تعالى سبب موجب لاستحقاق العاصي العذاب، وإذا كان ضده موجباً للعقاب كان الجانب الموافق واجباً؛ لأنّ امتناع أحد الجانبين مستلزم لوجوب الآخر وكذا وجوب أحدهما لامتناع الآخر، وحاصل التحقيق أنّ لزوم امتثال أمر الأمر على المأمور المخاطب يكون على قدر اختيار الأمر على المأمور وعلوّه وقدرته عليه، وعلى قدر هذه الولاية يكون قدر استحقاق العقوبة في مخالفته، فإذا كان مخالفة أمر المولى موجبةً لاستحقاق عقوبة عظيمة في حق عبده مع أنه لم يخلقه ولم يوجد بدنه ونفسه وأعضاؤه والنعم السابقة واللاحقة، وإنما ملكه رقبة ملكاً ناقصاً غير حقيقي ومع ذلك هو قابل الزوال والفناء، كان مخالفة أمر الله تعالى «وهو مالك ذرّات العالم وخالقها ومالكها ملكاً تاماً حقيقيَّاً» موجبةً لاستحقاق العقوبة بالطريق الأولى، فيكون موجب أمره هو الوجوب؛ لأنّ معنى الوجوب هاهنا أعم من الفرض والوجوب، ولا عقاب إلا في تركها فتذكر، كذا في بعض الحواشي.
(5) قوله: [بقدر ولاية الآمر يعني : إذا كان الأمر عالياً كان الايتمار واجباً، وإذا كان مساوياً يكون مندوباً، وإذا كان سافلاً لا يكون واجباً ولا مندوباً بل مباحاً، كذا في "غاية التحقيق".
Chapter on the disagreement of people regarding the absolute command, i.e., the scholars of the ummah from the mujtahids. Chapter: People disagreed regarding the absolute command, i.e., free from any evidence indicating necessity or non-necessity, like His statement: 'And when the Quran is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy' [7:204], and His statement: 'And do not approach this tree, lest you be among the wrongdoers' [2:35]. The correct view from the madhhab is that its requirement is obligation unless evidence to the contrary is established, because abandoning a command is disobedience just as obeying is obedience. Al-Hamasī said: 'I obeyed your commanders in severing my bond, order them to do the same to their loved ones. If they obey you, then obey them; and if they disobey you, then disobey the one who disobeys you.' And disobedience regarding the right of the shari'ah is a cause for punishment. Its verification is that the necessity of compliance is only according to the authority of the commander over the addressee. Therefore, if you direct the command form to one who is not obligated to obey you at all, it does not necessitate compliance. But if you direct it to one who is obligated to obey you, like slaves, compliance is necessitated, such that if he abandons it intentionally, he deserves punishment customarily and legally. Based on this, we know that the necessity of compliance is according to the authority of the commander. (1) He said: [People disagreed...etc.] So Ibn al-Sharih from the followers of al-Shafi'i went to the view that its requirement is suspension (tawaqquf) because it is used for many meanings, some of which are agreed-upon haqiqah. Thus when it is absolute, it has many probabilities, and probability necessitates suspension until the intended meaning is clarified. These include: recommendation (nadb) like '…then write for them' [24:33], reprimand like 'Do whatever you will' [41:40], challenge like 'Then bring a surah like it' [2:23], guidance like 'And call to witness' [2:282], gratitude like 'Eat from what Allah has provided you' [5:88], honor like 'Enter it in peace' [15:46], discipline like 'Eat from what is in front of you', subjugation like 'Be apes', humiliation like 'Taste! Indeed you are the mighty, the noble', supplication like 'Our Lord, forgive me', contempt like 'Throw what you will throw' [26:43] and others. Then know that according to him the suspension is in determining the intended meaning upon usage, not in determining the subject matter (wad'), because according to him it is placed (as a homonym) for obligation, recommendation, permissibility, and threat. Al-Ghazali and a group of verifiers went to suspension in determining the subject matter: whether it is only obligation, only recommendation, or homonymous between them. The generality of scholars are that it is specific to a particular meaning. Some said it is for recommendation because it is placed for requesting the action, and the least that tilts the side of existence is recommendation. The correct statement is that it is for obligation, as mentioned in the text. This is a summary from books of usul. (2) He said: [obeyed you] meaning they carried out your command, and carrying out the command is called obedience. So if they obey you in cutting off their love from the beloved, then obey them, i.e., carry out the command of your commander in severing my bond. (3) He said: [then disobey] i.e., abandon the command of one who abandoned your command, and abandoning the command is called disobedience. (4) He said: [And its verification...etc.] i.e., the verification that the requirement of the absolute command, while disregarding the requirements of diverting evidence and specific contexts, is obligation. And since violating the command is called disobedience in custom, the requirement and implication of the command is obligation. Because disobedience to Allah is a cause for deserving punishment. If the opposite is a cause for punishment, then the conforming side is obligatory, because the impossibility of one side necessitates the obligation of the other, and vice versa. The conclusion is that the necessity of compliance with the command of the commander upon the commanded addressee depends on the extent of the commander's authority over the addressee, his highness and power over him. According to this authority is the extent of deserved punishment for violating it. If violating the command of a master (mawla) necessitates deserving great punishment for his slave, even though he did not create him nor bring into existence his body, soul, limbs, and past and future blessings, and only owned him as an incomplete, non-genuine ownership that is subject to decay and annihilation, then violating the command of Allah—who is the owner of the atoms of the universe, their Creator, and their Owner with complete, genuine ownership—necessitates deserving punishment by a greater priority. Thus the requirement of His command is obligation, because the meaning of obligation here is more general than fard and wajib, and there is no punishment except in abandoning it. So remember. This is from some marginal notes. (5) He said: [according to the authority of the commander] meaning: if the commander is of high status, compliance is obligatory; if he is equal, it is recommendable; if he is of lower status, it is neither obligatory nor recommendable but permissible. So in 'Ghayat al-Tahqiq'.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute (ikhtilaf) about the absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author defines the absolute command as one free from any evidence indicating whether it requires obligation or not. He provides Quranic examples: the command to listen and be silent when the Quran is recited (7:204), and the command to Adam and Hawwa not to approach the tree (2:35). The correct view in the Hanafi madhhab is that the absolute command indicates obligation (wujub) unless proof indicates otherwise. The proof is that leaving a command is termed disobedience (ma'siya) and obedience is termed obedience (ta'a). The author quotes al-Hamasi's poetry to illustrate the customary understanding that disobeying a valid command deserves punishment. The verification: the necessity of compliance depends on the authority (wilaya) of the commander. If the commander has authority over the addressee (e.g., a master over a slave), then the command must be obeyed; otherwise not. Then the main footnote (1) details the positions: Ibn al-Sharih (a Shafi'i) holds that the absolute command requires suspension (tawaqquf) because it has many possible meanings, so we must pause to determine which is intended. Others (like al-Ghazali) suspend in determining the subject matter. The generality of scholars say the command is for a specific meaning; some say for recommendation (nadb) because it is a request for the action, and the minimal preference for existence is nadb. The correct position (as in the text) is that it is for obligation. Footnotes 2-5 further explain the poetry and the verification of the ruling, emphasizing that the degree of obedience required parallels the commander's authority, and that for Allah, the most supreme authority, the command necessarily indicates obligation. The final note distinguishes that when the commander is higher authority, the command is obligatory; when equal, it is recommendable; when lower, it is permissible. This shows how the Hanafi school uses reason (talil) and custom (urf) to derive the ruling.

Key Terms
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-mutlaq): The command form that is free from any evidence indicating whether the commanded act is obligatory or not.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The legal classification that an act is required; the absolute command necessitates it.
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspension of judgment until the intended meaning is clarified; the position of Ibn al-Sharih.
الايتمار (al-i'timār): Compliance with or acting according to a command; synonym for obedience.
المعصية (al-ma'siya): Disobedience; leaving a command without excuse. In custom, it is a cause for punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab as stated in the text, what is the default ruling of an absolute command (amr mutlaq) when no qualifying evidence is present?
A) It requires suspension (tawaqquf) until the intended meaning is clarified.
B) It indicates recommendation (nadb).
C) It indicates obligation (wujub).
D) It indicates permissibility (ibaha).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, an absolute command (amr mutlaq) indicates obligation (wujub) because violating it is called disobedience (ma‘ṣiya), which deserves punishment.

The Absolute Command and Its Implication of Obligation: The Authority Principle
Rational argument establishing the obligation of the absolute command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that an absolute command from a superior authority implies obligation (wujub) and identify the degree of obligation based on the commander's authority.

Core Idea
The binding force of a command depends on the authority of the commander over the addressee.

This lesson unpacks a foundational rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) from a superior authority obligates compliance. We will trace the rational proof (dalil aqli) provided by Imam al-Shashi and see how the degree of authority determines whether a command is wajib, mandub, or mubah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule: Absolute Command Implies Obligation
The passage states that the necessity of compliance (luzum al-aytamar) is proportional to the authority (wilaya) of the commander. An absolute command from a superior generates an obligation.
This is the core principle: authority determines bindingness.
2
Follow the Rational Proof from Human Master to Divine Lord
The argument uses a qiyas (analogy): if a human master's command binds a slave (with incomplete ownership), then Allah's command, who has complete, genuine ownership over everything, binds even more strongly.
The proof is a fortiori (awla).
3
Apply the Distinction of Authority Levels
The hashia clarifies: if the commander is higher (like Allah), compliance is wajib; if equal, mandub; if lower, mubah. This shows the principle scales with authority.
This is the practical outcome of the rule.
4
Note the Scope: All Commands in Shari'ah
This principle applies to every command (amr) in the Quran and Sunnah. The default for an absolute command from Allah is wujub.
It is a foundational maxim in Hanafi istinbat.

Source Text

فصل في اختلاف الناس في الأمر المطلق
أي آمروك. ١٢
بصرم جلال

(19) -
Chapter on the disagreement of people regarding the absolute command. [Marginal notes: various explanations]. The disobedience related to the right of the Shari'ah is a cause for punishment, and its verification: that the necessity of compliance with the command of the commander upon the addressed subordinate is in proportion to the authority of the commander over the subordinate, and for this reason, if the command form is directed to one who is not obligated to obey you at all, it does not necessitate compliance; but if you direct it to one who is obligated to obey you, such as slaves, then compliance is necessarily required, so much so that if he abandons it intentionally, he deserves punishment customarily and legally. Thus, we know that the necessity of compliance is proportional to the authority. (Commentary on 'they obeyed you': they complied with your command. Commentary on 'disobey': abandon the command of one who abandoned your command, and abandoning a command is called disobedience. Commentary on 'verification': that the implication of the absolute command, disregarding contextual significations and particularities of subject matter, is obligation, and since disobedience to the command is customarily called sin, the implication of the command and its requirement is obligation; because disobedience to Allah is a cause that obligates deserving punishment, and if its opposite is a cause for punishment, then the conforming side is obligatory; because the forbidding of one side necessitates the obligation of the other. The summary of the verification: that the necessity of compliance with the command of the commander upon the addressed subordinate is in proportion to the commander's authority over the subordinate, his highness, and his power over him, and according to this authority is the degree of deserving punishment in opposing him. So if opposing the command of a master obligates deserving great punishment for his slave—while he did not create him nor bring into existence his body, self, limbs, and preceding and subsequent blessings, and only owned his neck with an incomplete, non-genuine ownership that is subject to passing and annihilation—then opposing the command of Allah, who is the owner of the atoms of the world, their Creator, and their Owner with complete genuine ownership, obligates deserving punishment by the primary way. So his command necessitates obligation, because the meaning of obligation here is more general than fard and wajib, and there is no punishment except in abandoning it. Commentary on 'according to the authority of the commander' means: if the commander is high, compliance is wajib; if equal, it is mandub; if low, it is neither wajib nor mandub but mubah. Then the text continues: The authority of the commander, if this is established, we say: Allah is a complete owner in every part of the world, and He has disposal however He wills. And if it is established that the one with deficient ownership over a slave had abandoning compliance as a cause for punishment, then what do you think about abandoning the command of the One who created you from non-existence and poured upon you the showers of blessings? (Commentary on 'created you': He brought you into existence from the darkness of non-existence. And there is a possible objection answered. Commentary on 'showers of blessings': the first of rain, meaning the first and noblest of blessings.)
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi discusses the nature of the absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) and the dispute among scholars regarding its implication. The author begins by stating that the necessity of complying with a command is proportional to the authority of the commander over the addressee. If the commander has full authority (like a master over a slave), then compliance is obligatory (wajib); if the authority is equal, compliance is recommended (mandub); if the commander is lower, compliance is merely permissible (mubah). The hashia (commentary) provides a detailed verification (tahqiq) that the absolute command from Allah, who has complete and genuine ownership and power, necessarily implies obligation (wujub). The rationale is that disobedience to Allah incurs punishment, and since punishment is only for abandoning an obligation, the command must be obligatory. This is a foundational usul principle in the Hanafi school: the absolute command from a superior authority creates a binding obligation. The passage also addresses potential metaphysical objections regarding creation from non-existence, showing the depth of analysis typical of Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-mutlaq): The command considered without any accompanying contextual or particularizing factors; its default implication in Hanafi usul is obligation.
الولاية (al-wilaya): The authority or power of the commander over the addressee; it determines the degree of obligation (wajib, mandub, mubah).
الوجوب (al-wujud): The legal classification that compliance with the command is required and omission is punishable; encompasses both fard and wajib in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the default implication of an absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) from a superior authority?
A) It is permissible (mubah).
B) It is recommended (mandub).
C) It is obligatory (wajib).
D) It depends on the intention of the commander.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, an absolute command from a superior authority automatically places a binding obligation (wujub) on the addressee, and the degree of bindingness varies with the level of authority.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
The Imperative Does Not Imply Repetition: A Hanafi Usul Rule
Explaining the rule that an imperative only requires a single performance, with linguistic proof and fiqh applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that al-amr la yaqtadi al-takrar to derive the minimal obligation from a command, and distinguish the conditions under which a command may be interpreted as requiring repetition.

Core Idea
An unqualified command requires a single performance — the default is one, not many.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi, with the commentary Ahsan al-Hawashi, establishes one of the cornerstones of Hanafi usul: a command (amr) to perform an act does not imply repetition. The proof is linguistic: the imperative is an abbreviation of an expression containing the masdar (verbal noun), which is a noun of genus (ism al-jins). Genus nouns apply to the smallest instance by default but can accommodate the entire genus only by intention. We will walk through the linguistic argument and its applications to oaths, divorce, and marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its scope
State the rule: 'al-amr bi al-fi'l la yaqtadi al-takrar'. What does it govern? Commands to perform acts (not prohibitions or matters of belief). Understand that this is a default rule that can be overridden by context.
This rule is specific to the Hanafi school; the Shafi'i school holds that a command implies repetition unless evidence indicates otherwise.
2
Understand the linguistic proof
The author argues that the imperative form (e.g., 'idrib') is an abbreviation of a longer expression ('if'al fi'la al-darb'). Abbreviation does not change the legal meaning. The masdar ('darb') is a noun of genus (ism al-jins), which by default covers a single instance (al-fard al-haqiqi).
This linguistic analysis distinguishes between the imperative's form and its implied content.
3
Distinguish real vs. legal individual
Al-fard al-haqiqi is a single unit (e.g., one divorce). Al-fard al-hukmi is a legal composite treated as a unit by intention (e.g., three divorces as one complete talaq). The command defaults to the real individual, but intention can make it cover the whole genus as a legal individual.
Intending two (e.g., two divorces) is invalid because it is pure number, not a real or legal individual.
4
Apply to fiqh examples
Apply the rule: In oaths, 'I will not drink water' — breaking with any drop, because 'water' as ism al-jins includes the smallest amount. In divorce, 'talliqi nafsaki' — one divorce unless three intended. In marriage, master saying 'tazawwaj' to slave — one woman unless two intended (maximum for a slave).
The examples show how the same command yields different results based on the legal capacity of the subject (free vs. slave).
5
Address counter-examples
Commands like the five daily prayers seem to require repetition. The text notes that a contextual indicator (qarīna) can override the default. In this case, the context of the revelation and the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) indicate repetition.
The default is one; the burden of proof is on those who claim repetition.

أي إيجاده وفعله متحققا. فصل الأمر بالفعل لا يقتضي التكرار والاختصار في المرة الواحدة. كما أن قوله ضرب مختصر من فعل فعل الضرب في الزمان الماضي. طلب تحقيق الفعل على سبيل الاختصار فإن قوله: «اضرب» مختصر من لأن المصدر اسم الجنس. أي في إثبات الحكم وإفادته. قوله: «افعل فعل الضرب والمختصر من الكلام والمطول سواء في الحكم، هو الضرب. الأمر بالضرب أمر بجنس تصرف معلوم، وحكم اسم الجنس أن أي مجموع أفراده وجوداته في ضمنها. أي الواحد. الأدنى عند الإطلاق ويحتمل كل الجنس، يتناول القليل والكثير. يوجب التكرار حقيقةً ولكن يحتمله مجازاً عند قيام قرينة، والصحيح أنه لا يوجب التكرار حقيقته ولا يحتمله، كذا في "المعدن". (۱) قوله: [مختصر] فإن قيل: ما ذكرتم أنّ لفظ المصدر مختصر يستلزم التسلسل؛ لأنّ «اضرب» مختصر من «افعل فعل الضرب»، و«افعل» أيضاً أمر، فيقتضي أن يكون مختصراً من أمر آخر إلى أن تسلسل وهو باطل، قيل: التسلسل إنّما يبطل في سلسلة التوقف، وأما في سلسلة الأمور الاعتبارية كتضعيف الأعداد فلا. (۲) قوله: [فعل الضرب] كما أنّ «ضرب» مختصر من «فعل فعل الضرب في الماضي» و«يضرب» مختصر من «يفعل فعل الضرب في الزمان الآتي». (۳) قوله: [سواء في الحكم] أي في إثبات الحكم أو في إفادة المعنى؛ لأنّ فائدة الاختصار تقليل اللفظ لا تغيير المعنى المطول. (۴) قوله: [بجنس تصرف... إلخ الفرق بين الجنس واسم الجنس أن إطلاق اسم الجنس على الفرد بطريق الحقيقة ولا يطلق على الكثير بل على فرد فرد بطريق البدلية كالرجل والفرس والجنس على القليل والكثير على السواء كـ«الماء» يطلق على القطرة والبحر، وعلى هذا يكون كلّ جنس بدون العكس، فكان بينهما عموماً وخصوصاً مطلقاً. (۵) قوله: [ويحتمل كل الجنس] أي: عند النية؛ وذلك لأنّ اسم الجنس اسم فرد ليس بصيغة جمع ولا عدد، واسم الفرد لا يحتمل العدد والتكرار لما بين الفرد والعدد منافاة؛ إذ الفرد ليس فيه تركيب، والعدد بالعكس وبينهما تناف، فيراد به الماهية في ضمن الفرد، لكنّ الفرد قد يكون حقيقياً كالواحد من الجنس فإنه فرد حقيقةً وقد يكون حكمياً كالجنس بتمامه فإنه فرد حكماً وإن كان عدداً حقيقةً فيجعل الجنس بمنزلة شيء واحد فقلنا: إذا لم ينو شيئاً ينصرف إلى الواحد؛ لأنه فرد حقيقةً وحكماً إذا نوى الكل؛ لأنه نوى الفرد الحكمي فإذا نوى الأكثر من الفرد الحقيقي وأقل من الفرد الحكمي وهو القدر المتخلل بين الأدنى والكل لا يصح منه؛ لأنه عدد محض ليس فيه معنى الفردية بوجه فلا تقع عليه صيغة الفرد، كذا في "المعدن".
وعلى هذا قلنا: إذا حلف لا يشرب الماء، يحنث بشرب أدنى قطرة منه، ولو جمع ماء. فلا يحنث أصلا فإنه متعذر. نوی به جميع مياه العالم صحت نيته، ولهذا قلنا إذا قال لها: «طلقى نفسك» أي لتناول المذكور. أي لامرأته نفسي. الطلقة. الزوج. بقوله طلقي نفسك. الزوج. الزوج. فقالت: «طلقت» يقع الواحدة، ولو نوى الثلاث صحت نيته، وكذلك لو قال أي امرأتي. الطلقة. بقوله طلقها. أي مثل الحلف بقوله لا يشرب. الزوج. لآخر: «طلقها» يتناول الواحدة عند الإطلاق، ولو نوى الثلاث صحت نيته الطلقتين. (۳) للغير تزوجها وليس تحته حرة. لأنه عدد محض وليس كل الجنس. ولو نوى الثنتين لا يصح إلا إذا كانت المنكوحة أمة فإن نية الثنتين في حقها أي جنس طلاقها. المولى. نية بكل الجنس، ولو قال لعبده: «تزوج» يقع على تزوج امرأة واحدة، ولو المولى. نوى الثنتين صحت نيته؛ لأن ذلك كل الجنس في حق العبد، ولا يتأتى على أي مجموعهما. أي جنس المنكوحات. تزوج الامرأتين. (۱) قوله: [وعلى هذا] أي: على أنّ اسم الجنس يتناول الأدنى عند الإطلاق ويحتمل كل الجنس. (۲) قوله: [ولهذا ... إلخ] أي: ولأنّ الأمر لا يقتضي التكرار ولا يحتمله، قلنا: في قول الرجل: «طلق نفسك» إنه يقع على الواحدة إن لم ينو شيئاً أو نوى واحدةً أو نوى ثنتين، وإن نوى ثلاثاً فعلى ما نوى؛ لأنّ الواحد فرد حقيقي متيقن والثلاث فرد حكمي محتمل، ولا تصح نية الثنتين؛ لأنه عدد محض ليس بفرد حقيقي ولا حكمي وليس مدلول اللفظ ولا محتملاً له، إلا إذا كانت تلك المرأة أمة؛ لأنّ الثنتين في حقها كالثلاثة في حق الحرة فهو واحد حكمي كالثلاث في حقها. (۳) قوله : لا يصح حاصل الفرق أنّ المصدر الذي دلّ عليه الفعل فرد مع كونه جنساً والفرد يصلح كلّ أفراد الجنس؛ لأنّ الضرب الذي دلّ عليه قوله : «اضرب» مثلاً الجمع أفراد جنس واحد من الضربات، وكذا الطلاق في قوله: «طلق» وأمّا المثنى فعدد محض وليس بفرد لا حقيقةً ولا حكماً فلا يحتمله والنيّة إنّما يحتمل فيما احتمله اللفظ، إلا إذا كانت المنكوحة أمة الغير تزوجها ليست تحته حرة، فح تصح نية الثنتين؛ لأنّ كلّ أفراد الجنس طلاقها ثنثان، كذا في "الفصول". ( قوله: [كلّ الجنس... إلخ ] ثُمّ الكلّ على ثلاثة معان: كلّ كلّي، وكلّ مجموعي، وكلّ أفرادي، فالأوّل بمعنى الماهية نحو: كلّ إنسان نوع، والثاني بمعنى المجموع نحو : كلّ إنسان لا يسعه الدار، والثالث بمعنى الفرد نحو: كلّ إنسان يشبعه هذا الرغيف. (5) قوله: [ولا يتأتى... إلخ] جواب سؤال يرد على المذهب الصحيح: وهو أن الأوامر بالصلوات الخمس والصيام والزكاة موجبة لها على سبيل التكرار ولهذا تكرّر وجوب العبادات، وتقرير الجواب بناء على أي الأمة المنكوحة.
A person who swears he will not drink water breaks his oath by drinking the smallest drop of it, even if it is but a little water. If he intended all the waters of the world, his intention is valid. Likewise, if a husband says to his wife: 'Divorce yourself,' it refers to the mentioned [woman]. She says: 'I divorced myself,' and one divorce occurs. If he intended three, his intention is valid. Similarly, if he says to another [woman]: 'Divorce her,' it applies to one when unqualified, and if he intended three, his intention for the two divorces is valid. For one who intends to marry a slave woman not having a free woman under him, intending two is not valid unless the married woman is a slave, for in her case the intention of two is like all of her genus of divorce. If a master says to his slave: 'Marry,' it applies to one woman, and if he intended two, his intention is valid because that is all of the genus with respect to the slave, and it is not possible to marry two women [simultaneously].
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi, with the commentary Ahsan al-Hawashi, discusses the legal principle that a command (amr) to perform an act does not require repetition (takrar). The author establishes this by analyzing the linguistic nature of the masdar (verbal noun) and its relationship to the verb. The argument is that the imperative form is an abbreviation of a longer expression, and abbreviation does not change the meaning. The masdar is a noun of genus (ism al-jins) which inherently covers both the minimal instance and the entire genus, but when used in an unqualified command, it defaults to a single occurrence (al-fard al-haqiqi). The commentary addresses potential objections, such as infinite regress, and provides detailed explanations of the differences between 'jins' and 'ism al-jins', and between a real individual and a legal individual (fard hukmi). The rule is applied to practical examples in oaths (yamin), divorce (talaq), and marriage (nikah). For oaths, the command 'do not drink water' is broken by any amount, even a drop, because the genus of water includes the smallest quantity. For divorce, if a husband says to his wife 'divorce yourself', it results in one divorce unless he intends three (which is possible as a legal individual). Intending two is invalid because it is pure number and not a real or legal individual. However, if the wife is a slave, twice is the maximum (like three for a free woman) so the intention of two is valid as a legal individual. Similarly, for a master telling his slave to marry, it applies to one woman or two (the maximum for a slave) but not more. The commentary also briefly addresses the objection that commands like the five daily prayers are repeated; the answer (not fully detailed here) is that the context of those commands indicates repetition.

Key Terms
التكرار (al-takrār): Repetition of an act required by a command; the text holds that a command does not require repetition.
اسم الجنس (ism al-jins): A noun that refers to a genus, applicable to both few and many, e.g., 'water' for a drop or an ocean.
الفرد الحقيقي (al-fard al-ḥaqīqī): A true individual, e.g., one divorce or one performance of an act.
الفرد الحكمي (al-fard al-ḥukmī): A legal individual considered as a single unit even if containing multiple parts, e.g., three divorces as one 'unit' of complete talaq, or all waters of the world as one genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule discussed, if a man swears 'I will not drink water' and then drinks a single drop, does he break his oath?
A) No, because a drop is not considered drinking.
B) Yes, because the genus of water includes the smallest amount.
C) Only if he drinks a full glass.
D) Only if he intended to drink all the world's water.

Key Takeaway
An unqualified command (amr) obligates a single performance; repetition requires a contextual indicator (qarīna).

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Al-Amr La Yaqtadi al-Tikrar: The Command Does Not Imply Repetition
Commentary expanding the principle with legal examples and worship application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a command takes the minimum individual and does not require repetition, and distinguish between the cause of obligation and the performance of obligation to resolve the apparent repetition in daily worship.

Core Idea
A command demands the minimum once; repetition comes from repeated causes, not from the command itself.

This lesson explores the foundational Hanafi usul principle that a command (amr) for an action does not inherently imply repetition (tikrar). Through examples from oaths, divorce, and marriage, the text explains that the command covers the minimum individual of the genus, but also potentially the entire genus if intended. Later, it resolves a crucial challenge: how can daily prayers be obligatory repeatedly if commands don't imply repetition? The answer lies in the Hanafi distinction between the obligation itself (wujub al-asl) and the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada) — the command demands performance of an obligation already established by a separate cause (sabab).

Guided Path
1
Grasp the principle: command takes the minimum of the genus
The passage begins by stating that the name of the genus (jins) when unrestricted takes the minimum individual, though it potentially covers all individuals. This means a command like 'drink water' is fulfilled with any amount, but it could be intended to mean all water.
This is the basis for the non-repetition rule: one act fulfills the command unless more is intended.
2
Apply the principle to oaths
If someone swears 'I will not drink water', the oath is broken by the smallest drop. That's because the genus 'water' includes every droplet. Even collecting all the water in the world, the oath would not be broken by that act alone because it is impossible to collect all. Intention of all waters is valid if sworn.
This shows the command/oath takes the minimum but can extend by intention.
3
Apply the principle to divorce and marriage
When a husband says 'Divorce yourself' to his wife, it applies to one divorce. If he intends three, it counts as three. But if he intends two, it does not work because two is a pure number and not an individual of the genus (divorce is a countable act). However, if the wife is a slave, two is like three for a free woman, so two is valid. Similarly, for marriage commands: 'Marry' defaults to one wife; for a slave who cannot marry a free woman, intending two wives is valid because all individuals of the genus for him are two.
This illustrates how the command's coverage interacts with intention and context.
4
Understand the three meanings of 'kull'
The passage introduces that 'kull' (all/entire) has three meanings: kull as universal (essence), e.g., 'Every human is a species'; kull as collective (totality), e.g., 'All humans cannot fit in a house'; and kull as distributive (each individual), e.g., 'Every human is satisfied by this loaf.' This linguistic distinction is crucial for interpreting commands that use 'all'.
This only applies when the word 'kull' appears; the basic command does not include 'kull' and therefore takes the minimum.
5
Resolve the worship repetition: distinguish obligation cause from performance
The text addresses a question: if commands don't imply repetition, why are daily prayers, fasting, and zakat commanded repeatedly? The Hanafi answer: the repetition is not from the command but from the repetition of their causes (asbab). For example, the time of each prayer is a new cause that establishes the obligation. The command then demands performance of that already established obligation (ada). This is analogous to a buyer being obliged to pay the price by the contract, but the command to pay comes later as a demand for performance. The command itself does not establish the obligation; it only demands performance.
This is the key Hanafi position that separates wujub al-asl from wujub al-ada, and it contrasts with the Shafi'i view that the command itself creates the obligation.

Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the principle that the command (amr) for an action does not imply repetition (la yaqtadi al-tikrar). The commentator explains that when a command is given, it refers to the minimum individual of the genus, but it potentially covers all individuals of the genus. This is illustrated with examples from oaths, divorce, and marriage. For instance, if someone swears not to drink water, even a single drop breaks the oath, because the genus 'water' includes any amount. In divorce, if a husband says 'Divorce yourself' to his wife, it applies to one divorce unless he intends more, because the command covers the minimum (one). Similarly, in marriage commands, the default is one wife unless the context changes (e.g., a slave can marry two with intention). The commentary then introduces a nuanced discussion about the word 'kull' (all), which has three meanings: universal (kulli), collective (majmu'i), and distributive (afradi). This is crucial for understanding how commands apply to multiple individuals. Later, the passage addresses an apparent conflict: commands for daily prayers, fasting, and zakat seem to require repetition. The commentator resolves this by distinguishing between the obligation itself (wujub al-asl) and the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada). The obligation itself is established by causes (e.g., time for prayer, month for fasting), not by the command. The command then demands performance of what is already obligatory due to those causes. Thus, repetition in worship arises from repeated causes, not from the command itself. This is a key Hanafi position contrasting with the Shafi'i view that the command (khatab) is the direct cause of obligation. The passage includes a detailed response to Shafi'i, asserting that the command is for performance of an already existing obligation, similar to demanding payment of a price after a sale or maintenance after marriage.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The command or directive from a superior to an inferior, which in usul al-fiqh does not imply repetition by itself.
التكرار (al-tikrar): Repeating an action multiple times. The passage argues that amr does not inherently demand repetition.
الجنس (al-jins): The genus or category that includes multiple individuals; the command takes the minimum individual but potentially covers the entire genus.
الكل (al-kull): The word 'all' with three meanings: kulli (universal as essence), majmu'i (collective totality), and afradi (each individual).
النية (al-niyya): Intention of the speaker, which can influence the scope of a command when the word potentially covers more than the minimum.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The obligation itself, distinguished from the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada). The obligation itself is established by cause (sabab).
الأداء (al-ada): Performance of an act that is already obligatory; the command demands performance, not the original obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the principle 'al-amr la yaqtadi al-tikrar', if a man says to his wife 'Anti taliq' (You are divorced) without any intention, how many divorces occur according to the Hanafi madhhab?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
The command itself takes the minimum individual of a genus and does not demand repetition; repetition in worship arises from repeated causes, not the command.

The Command Does Not Imply Repetition
Ruling and proof that a command does not necessitate repetition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a command does not imply repetition and distinguish between the obligation arising from the cause versus the command.

Core Idea
A command demands performance, not repetition; repetition comes from recurring causes, not the command itself.

One of the foundational debates in usul al-fiqh is whether a single command (amr) to perform an act demands that act be performed only once or repeatedly whenever its cause occurs. The Hanafi school holds a precise position: the command itself does not imply repetition; repetition is due to the recurrence of the cause (sabab) or time. This lesson unpacks that rule using the example of the daily Zuhr prayer, demonstrating how the rule applies across all obligatory acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed claim
Recognize the issue: does a command (e.g., 'pray') imply that the act must be performed multiple times (takrir)? Some scholars argue yes because the command is general and includes all instances. The Hanafi school denies this.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi response is that the command does not imply repetition. The proof: the command includes the entire genus (jins) of the obligatory act. When the cause (e.g., time) repeats, a new obligation arises, and the same command covers that new instance because it covers the genus.
The proof is not that the command itself demands repetition, but that its coverage of the genus allows it to apply to multiple instances triggered by causes.
3
Distinguish between command and cause
Understand that the obligation to perform an act at a specific time comes from the cause (e.g., the entry of Zuhr time), not from the command. The command is one and covers all instances of that genus that occur due to their causes.
For example, the command 'pray Zuhr' applies to today's Zuhr and tomorrow's Zuhr, but only because the cause (time) repeats, not because the command itself calls for repetition.
4
Derive the practical consequence
Apply the rule: if someone prays two Zuhr prayers on the same day, the second is invalid because the command was fulfilled once; no new cause arose. This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) between the Hanafi view and those who claim the command implies repetition.

Source Text

(1) He said: "Every genus of obligatory act upon him, whether fasting or prayer, over his lifetime which is a virtual individual; because the prayer of the obligated person is the entire genus of prayer relative to him, and the command has included the genus of prayer which is a virtual individual, so it is as if he is required to perform every Zuhr that is obligatory upon him in his lifespan at one time. And analogize other acts of worship accordingly." Quoted from al-Ma'din.

And what was mentioned first is a preamble, as mentioned later, or an indication of the difference between the very obligation established by the cause and the obligation of performance established by the command, in refutation of the one who claimed that they are the same meaning.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage addresses the usul rule: a command (amr) for an action does not imply that the action must be repeated multiple times. The text uses the example of Zuhr prayer: the command to pray Zuhr applies to each occurrence of the time (the cause), not because the command itself demands repetition. The commentary from al-Ma'din explains that the command covers the entire genus of the obligated act, so if a person lives for many years, the command effectively demands all those instances at once — but that is due to the recurring cause, not the command's inherent demand. The second footnote clarifies that the proof is based on the command including the genus, and it refutes those who conflate the obligation established by the cause with the obligation of performance established by the command.

Key Terms
التكرار (al-tikrār): The repetition of the commanded act
جنس الواجب (jins al-wājib): The genus or category of the obligatory act
السبب (al-sabab): The cause that triggers the obligation (e.g., entry of time)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A command does not necessitate repetition; the repetition of an obligatory act is due to the recurrence of its cause (time), not the command itself.

Types of Commanded Acts: Muwaqqat and Mutlaq
Classification of commanded acts and discussion on immediate vs delayed performance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muwaqqat and mutlaq commands, and apply the Hanafi rule that absolute commands do not require immediate performance (fawr).

Core Idea
Absolute commands permit delay; immediate performance is commendable but not obligatory.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces the fundamental classification of commanded acts into muwaqqat (time-bound) and mutlaq (absolute). The Hanafi school holds that absolute commands do not necessitate immediate performance, allowing the obligated person to delay without sin, as long as negligence is avoided. This ruling has significant practical implications for obligations like zakat, expiation, and voluntary acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of commanded acts
The author states that the commanded act (al-mamur bihi) is of two types: muwaqqat (time-bound) and mutlaq (absolute/unrestricted).
This classification is foundational in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
2
Understand the Hanafi principle on immediate performance
For absolute commands, the Hanafi view is that obligation is not immediate; one may delay without sin. Immediate performance is recommended (mandub).
This is the core difference with the Shafi'i school.
3
Review the evidence from zakat, sadaqat al-fitr, and 'ushr
These obligations are commanded in absolute terms (e.g., 'Give zakat') without a specific time. The text cites that delaying does not make one negligent.
If the wealth perishes after the year before payment, the obligation drops, proving that delay was not sinful.
4
Examine the practical consequence for oaths (expiation)
If one delays financial expiation and then becomes poor, he may expiate by fasting. Had immediate performance been obligatory, he would be held to financial expiation despite poverty.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) between the Hanafi and opposing views.
5
Contrast with the opposing view and note the dispute
The passage acknowledges that some schools (likely Shafi'i) argue that absolute commands demand immediacy. The Hanafi response is that the command itself does not specify time, and the examples show delay is permitted.
Both schools agree that haste is virtuous, but they differ on whether delay incurs sin.

And from al-Karkhi, may Allah have mercy on him: the requirement of the command is knowledge, that one knows by dominant probability that delaying to a time when he does not think it will be lost, and such probability suffices as knowledge. Sudden death is rare and not suitable for building rulings upon. 12. (1) His statement: [he does not become...] etc. due to the absolute command for zakat, which is His statement: 'And give zakat' (al-Baqarah:43), and for sadaqat al-fitr, his statement (peace be upon him): 'Give from every free person and slave', and for 'ushr, his statement (peace be upon him): 'What is watered by the sky, one-tenth', and each of these is unrestricted in time, so he does not become negligent by delaying, i.e., not blameworthy, as in al-Fusul. 12. (2) His statement: [for if the wealth perishes...] Evidence that the obligated person does not become negligent by delaying; for if the nisab perishes after the full year before paying zakat, the obligation drops from his responsibility and he does not sin. Had he been negligent in delaying payment, the obligation would remain and he would sin for the delay, as in al-Ma'dan. 12. (3) His statement: [if his wealth goes...] Means: the one who breaks an oath is initially commanded with financial expiation when he has wealth, and with fasting when he lacks it, as per His statement: 'Its expiation is feeding ten needy people from the average of what you feed your families, or clothing them, or freeing a neck. But whoever cannot find [the means] then fasting of three days' (al-Ma'idah:89). And the command for financial expiation is absolute, so if he delays it while possessing wealth, he is not negligent. So if his wealth goes and he becomes poor, he expiates by fasting and is not held to financial expiation, because he was not negligent in delaying. Had the absolute command been immediate, he would have been held to financial expiation and fasting would not have sufficed, and he would have been negligent in delaying, as in some hashiyah. 12. Assembly: 'al-Madinah al-'Ilmiyyah' (al-Da'wah al-Islamiyyah) (97) -

(1) His statement: 'on immediacy' i.e., immediately without delay, and the interpretation of immediacy is that one must hasten performance after the command is issued at the first possible time, so he sins by delaying beyond the first possible time, but he sins a suspended sin such that if he performs later, that sin is lifted, as in al-Ma'dan. 12. (2) His statement: 'a container for the act' i.e., for performing the commanded act. Then 'container' in language is a general term that includes a container that is surplus to the content and a container that is not surplus, but the term in the terminology of the imams of usul is specifically for every container that is surplus to the content, like the time of prayer. That which is not surplus to the content and the content is not surplus to it is called a standard, like the time of fasting, as in al-Ma'dan. 12. (3) His statement: 'even if...' The evidence for its being a container is that it is not required to occupy the entire time with prayer, and the interpretation of the container nature of time here is that the act can occur with the possibility of surplus beyond the performance. 12. (4) His statement: 'does not preclude the validity of another prayer' If it is said: This ruling is redundant because the obligation of something entails its validity, it is said: It is possible for the obligatory to conflict with another obligatory, and supererogatory is not valid alongside it. And his statement: 'validity of another prayer' is absolute, including supererogatory and obligatory. Then his confirmation i.e., the author's confirmation by his statement: 'even if he occupied the entire time of zuhr' does not match this ruling; because what results is the loss of zuhr by occupying time with something else, and the confirmed (mut'aqqid) combines performing zuhr and the missed prayer, which does not suit the performed act, and suitability is a condition between the confirmer and confirmed. But we say in response: The word 'even' here is not for exaggeration, for the extreme is used for exaggeration in the action in extension and intensity, as you say: 'I will strike you until I kill you.' It is for confirmation. 12. Assembly: 'al-Madinah al-'Ilmiyyah' (al-Da'wah al-Islamiyyah) 97 (qv) —
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi, with its commentary, deals with the classification of commanded acts (al-mamur bih). The author begins by stating that the commanded act is of two types: muwaqqat (time-bound) and mutlaq (absolute/unrestricted in time). The commentary expands on this by providing examples from fiqh: if one vows to perform i'tikaf for a month, he may choose any month, because the command is absolute; similarly for fasting. The discussion then focuses on the ruling regarding delay in performing absolute commands. The Hanafi position is that absolute commands do not necessitate immediate performance (fawr); rather, they permit delay (tarakhi) as long as one is not negligent. Evidence is drawn from zakat, sadaqat al-fitr, and 'ushr, where the command is unrestricted in time, so delaying does not make one sinful. If the wealth perishes before payment, the obligation drops, indicating that one was not culpable. Similarly, for an oath, if one delays financial expiation until he becomes poor, he may expiate by fasting. The commentary notes that the dispute (with some other schools, likely Shafi'i) is about whether the obligation is immediate; there is no disagreement that hastening is commendable. The passage then distinguishes between two types of time-bound commands: those where time is a 'container' (zarf) that is surplus to the act (like prayer, where the entire time need not be filled), and those where time is a 'standard' (mi'yar) that must be fully occupied (like fasting). For container-type time, the obligation of one act does not preclude the validity of another act in the same time. The footnote clarifies that 'even if he occupied the entire time of zuhr' is used for confirmation, not exaggeration. Overall, the passage lays out the usul principle that absolute commands allow delay, and clarifies the nature of time in relation to obligations.

Key Terms
المأمور به (al-mamur bihi): The commanded act; the act that is the object of a legislative command.
الفور (al-fawr): Immediate performance of a commanded act at the first possible time.
التراخي (al-tarakhi): Delaying performance of an act beyond the first possible time, which is permitted unless specified otherwise.
الوقت (al-waqt): The time designated for an act; can be a container (zarf) or a standard (mi'yar).
الظرف (al-zarf): A time that is greater than what is needed for the act, allowing other acts.
المعيار (al-mi'yar): A time that is exactly the measure of the act, not allowing surplus (like fasting from dawn to sunset).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, what happens to the obligation of zakat if the nisab is destroyed after one year but before payment?
A) The obligation remains, and one must pay from other wealth.
B) The obligation drops, and one is not sinful for delaying.
C) The obligation becomes a debt on the person.
D) One must make up the payment after acquiring wealth again.

Key Takeaway
Absolute commands in Hanafi usul permit delay (tarakhi); immediate performance is commendable but not obligatory, and delay does not incur sin as long as negligence is avoided.
