Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
The Two Types of Commanded Acts: Container-Time vs Measure-Time
Definition and rule with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any time-bound commanded act as either container-time or measure-time and apply the corresponding Hanafi ruling on intention.

Core Idea
Time either contains an act alongside others or exclusively measures it, changing the need for specific intention.

In Hanafi usul, commanded acts (ma'mur bihi) relate to time in two fundamentally different ways. This classification determines when a specific intention is required and when the Lawgiver's designation suffices. Understanding these two categories is essential for correctly applying rulings on prayers, fasts, and other time-bound obligations. This lesson unpacks the definition, the inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i, and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of time relation
Identify whether the time period can accommodate other acts (container) or is exclusive to the commanded act (measure). Examples: Zuhr prayer time is container; Ramadan day is measure.
The key is whether non-commanded acts are legally valid in that time.
2
Apply intention rule for container-time
When time is a container, specific intention (ta'yin al-niyya) is required initially because competitors exist. If time narrows to exclude others, intention may be waived.
Competition remains until the last moment, so intention is still needed unless time is so narrow that no other act fits.
3
Apply intention rule for measure-time
When time is a measure (e.g., Ramadan), no other fast is valid in that time. Therefore, even if the worshiper intends a different obligation, the act counts for the designated one.
The Lawgiver's specification overrides the individual's intention.
4
Analyze the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on Ramadan fast
Al-Shafi'i requires specific intention even in exclusive time. The Hanafi response: the servant is already bound by the general command; specification from the Lawgiver suffices.
The thamarah (practical difference): in Hanafi, a vow fast in Ramadan fulfills Ramadan; in Shafi'i, it does not.
5
Identify exceptions (traveler/sick)
Abu Hanifa holds that a traveler or sick person, having concession to break the fast, can intend a different obligation in Ramadan because the exclusivity is removed for them.
This exception is based on the principle that concession for bodily need extends to religious obligations.

(1) His saying: 'except with a specific intention' meaning: except with an intention that specifies the commanded act personally or typologically such that the intended does not apply except to this commanded act, and it is not obligatory to specify the number of rak'ahs or to observe the meaning of performance or travel, etc. Specification in intention; because the container capacity accommodates others, and specification in intention repels the possible competitor that has legal validity, and this remains until the end of the time even if it becomes narrow and becomes a measure for it in reality, not from the perspective of the Law. Thus the validity of the competitor, i.e., other than the commanded act, remains; for if he prayed other than it, the prayer would be valid, so specification is also required. (2) His saying: 'and competition remains...' meaning: the competition of other than it, i.e., the validity of the non-time-bound act alongside it; because it is not specified for the commanded act even if the servant's option falls due to the narrowness of time. (3) His saying: 'as a measure for it' meaning: the measure of a thing is that by which the thing is measured; what is meant here is the time that the act occupies and does not exceed and is measured, lengthening with the length of time and shortening with its shortness, as in al-Ma'din. (4) His saying: 'nothing else is obligatory in that time' such that if one vows a fast in Ramadan, it is not obligatory to perform it, and therefore it is not permissible to perform it; because time does not accommodate two fasts, and it is not for him to change the law, so it is specified by what is legally obligatory, and nothing else is obligatory, as in al-Ma'din. (5) His saying: 'not for what he intended' because when the Lawgiver specified for the measure a fast with a description, if the responsible person wants to change the description, he cannot; this is like one who hires himself for a specific time for sewing, then sews for him and then intends charity in that time, it is not charity but rather hire. He only restricted with 'healthy resident' to exclude the traveler and the sick according to Abu Hanifa, for in his view if the sick or traveler intends in Ramadan for another obligation, it falls for what he intended; because since concession is established for them for bodily benefit (breaking fast), then it is more appropriate to establish it for religious benefit (fulfilling a debt), as in al-Fusul. (1) His saying: 'when the competitor is repelled' meaning that which is not this fast, i.e., it is not permissible in the time, and it is explained as impermissibility lest it be thought that the meaning of 'when the competitor is repelled' is the competitor in priority. (2) His saying: 'falls...' and according to al-Shafi'i, specification is required; because the specification came from the Lawgiver, so it must be specified by the servant so that he is a free chooser in that, not compelled. We say: the servant is free in such specification; because in the principle of fasting he is a free chooser, so he is addressed on this day for the fast, not for specification; because specification has come from the Lawgiver, so there is no need for the servant's specification, so preserve it. (3) His saying: 'the condition of specification' meaning: specification of the fast by attributing the fast to Ramadan, such that the responsible person says: 'I intend the fast of Ramadan.' (4) His saying: 'the condition of specification falls...' meaning: since the condition of specification was to cut off the competition in the time, when the competition is repelled, the condition of specification falls; because when it is specified in Ramadan, it is attained by the absolute name, and by mistake in the description, like one who is specified in a place, he is addressed with the genus name and type, just as he would be addressed with the proper name. For if 'Zaid' were called out 'O human' or 'O man' while he is alone in the house, it would be as if he was called 'O Zaid'; and he is addressed by mistake in the description, as if a white man alone in the house is called out 'O black man', and he is addressed by that; because 'black' is nullified, and the genus name that is suitable as a name for him remains, as is said. (5) His saying: 'and the basic intention does not fall...' is an answer to a question: that when the time is specified for the fast of Ramadan, it should be that the basic intention falls and is fulfilled without intention for the healthy resident. (6) His saying: 'it is abstention...' it is better to say: it is abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse, literally or legally intentionally from dawn to sunset, to exclude the eating, drinking, and intercourse of one who forgets, and the eating...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and rule-based exposition from the commentary on Usul al-Shashi. It classifies commanded acts (al-ma'mur bihi) into two types based on the relationship with time. The first type is that wherein time is a container (zarf) for the act, like the five daily prayers. In this type, the time period can accommodate both the commanded act and other acts (e.g., performing a missed prayer or supererogatory prayer within Zuhr time). Because other acts are legally valid in that time, the commanded act does not become specified (mutlaq) by the mere passage of time unless the time becomes very narrow (diq al-waqt). The ruling is that the commanded act must be performed with a specific intention (niyya mu'ayyana) to distinguish it from other possible acts, except when the time is so narrow that no other act can intervene, then specification is not required because the competition is removed. The second type is that wherein time is a measure (mi'yar) for the act, like fasting (sawm) in Ramadan. Here, the act itself is defined and measured by the time period: the fast extends and contracts with the length of the day. The Lawgiver has designated the entire time exclusively for this specific act; no other fast is valid within that time. Therefore, if a healthy resident intends a different obligation (e.g., a vow fast) during Ramadan, his fast automatically counts for Ramadan regardless of his intention. This is because the competition from other acts is absent; the time is exclusively for Ramadan. The commentary includes a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who requires specific intention even when time is exclusive, because the specification comes from the Lawgiver and the servant must consciously choose it. The Hanafi response is that the servant is already addressed with the generic obligation of fasting that day; the specification by the Lawgiver suffices, so no additional human specification is needed. The passage also notes an exception according to Abu Hanifa: a traveler or sick person, who has the concession to break the fast, may validly intend a different obligation in Ramadan because they are not bound by Ramadan's exclusive specification.

Key Terms
المأمور به (al-ma'mur bihi): The act commanded by the Lawgiver, which is the subject of the command.
الوقت ظرف له (al-waqt zarf lahu): Time that acts as a container, allowing other acts to be performed within it alongside the commanded act.
الوقت معيار له (al-waqt mi'yar lahu): Time that measures the act itself, exclusive to that act, not accommodating others.
تعيين النية (ta'yin al-niyya): Specification in intention to pinpoint which specific commanded act is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, if a healthy resident intends a different obligation (e.g., a make-up fast) during Ramadan, what is the ruling?
A) It counts for the intended obligation.
B) It counts for Ramadan, not for what he intended.
C) It is invalid without specific intention for Ramadan.
D) It is valid as a supererogatory fast.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when time is a measure (exclusive), the Lawgiver's specification overrides the individual's intention; specific intention is only required when time is a container with room for other acts.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Principle of Haqq al-'Abd vs. Haqq al-Shar' in Vows
Explaining the principle that the servant's specification only affects his own right, not the Shari'ah's ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between the servant's right and the Shari'ah's right to determine when a vow can alter a ruling.

Core Idea
A servant's specification affects only his own rights, not the Shari'ah's fixed rulings.

When a servant vows to fast a specific day, does that vow bind him absolutely, or can he override it with another obligation? The answer hinges on whether the matter involves a right of the servant or a right of the Shari'ah. This lesson unpacks that foundational usul principle using the example of supererogatory vows versus obligatory make-up fasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
Consider a person who vows to fast a specific day (e.g., a Friday). He later fasts that day to make up a missed Ramadan fast. Which obligation does the fast fulfill?
2
Distinguish the two rights
Recognize that the vow creates a personal obligation (haqq al-'abd), while the make-up of Ramadan is a fixed Shari'ah obligation (haqq al-shar'). The former is within the servant's control; the latter is not.
The Shari'ah has made the time for qada' and kaffarah absolute (mutlaq) – any day works.
3
Apply the rule
The servant's specification of a particular day cannot alter the absolute nature of the Shari'ah's ruling. Therefore, his act of fasting on that day fulfills the Shari'ah obligation first, not the vow.
Had he fasted solely for the vow with no other intention, the fast would be for the vow.
4
Test the exception
Consider the reverse: fasting a vowed day for a supererogatory (nafl) act. Since nafl is entirely a servant's right, the servant's specification (the vow) does affect it: the fast counts for the vow, not the nafl.
This confirms that the servant can modify his own rights but not the Shari'ah's.
5
Synthesise the principle
Conclude: whenever a servant imposes a restriction (e.g., a vow) that conflicts with a Shari'ah right, the Shari'ah right prevails. The servant's authority extends only over acts that are purely his own (like supererogatory worship).
This is a core Hanafi usul principle applicable across many fiqh chapters.

He forbade them from turning out and forbade them from going out. So it is known that staying in the house is the right of the Shari'ah, unlike maintenance, for it is obligatory in exchange for her surrendering herself to him and as compensation for her incapacitation from earning her livelihood, as in 'al-Ma'din'.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a fundamental usul principle: the distinction between what is a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd) and what is a right of the Shari'ah (haqq al-shar'). The author begins by examining the case of a vow (nazr) to fast. If a person vows to fast a specific day (e.g., a particular Friday), that vow becomes obligatory upon him. However, if he instead fasts that day for another obligation, such as making up missed Ramadan fasts or expiation for an oath, does the fast count for the vow or the other obligation? The ruling is that it counts for the other obligation, not the vow. The reason: the Shari'ah has made the time for making up missed fasts absolute (mutlaq) – i.e., any day. The servant's specification of a particular day cannot change that absolute ruling because the time for make-up is a right of the Shari'ah. The servant has no power to restrict it. The same applies to the fast of expiation, which is also absolute. The passage then addresses a possible objection: why does the same reasoning not apply to supererogatory (nafl) fasts? After all, supererogatory fasts are also absolute in time. So if one fasts a day that he vowed for nafl, but intends something else (like make-up), why does it count for the vow? The answer: supererogatory fasting is a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd). The Shari'ah has given the servant complete discretion over such acts. Therefore, the servant's specification does affect the supererogatory: by vowing to fast a specific day, he restricts the absolute nature of supererogatory fasting in that instance. So the act is dedicated to the vow. In other words, the servant can exercise his authority over his own rights, but he cannot tamper with the Shari'ah's rights. This principle is then applied to another area: the case of khul' (divorce initiated by the wife in return for compensation). The commentators (mashayikh) state that if the spouses stipulate in the khul' that the wife will have no maintenance (nafaqah) and no residence (suknah) during her 'iddah (waiting period), the maintenance drops but the residence does not. Why? Because maintenance during 'iddah is purely the wife's right (haqq al-'abd), so she can waive it. But residence (i.e., the right to stay in the marital home during the 'iddah) is a right of the Shari'ah (haqq al-shar'), established by explicit Quranic verses (al-Talaq 65:1,6). Neither spouse has the power to annul this right. Therefore, the husband cannot evict her, and she cannot leave (except for a clear immorality). The husband's inability to evict her is a consequence of the Shari'ah's command. The passage concludes with further discussion of the proof for the residence being a Shari'ah right: the Quranic prohibitions on eviction and leaving, combined with the fact that maintenance is in exchange for the wife's surrender and is thus a private right.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): Unrestricted in time; an absolute command without temporal specification.
مقيد (muqayyad): Restricted by a condition or specification; here, the vow restricts the otherwise absolute time of supererogatory fasting.
حق العبد (haqq al-'abd): A legal right that belongs to the individual; such rights can be waived or modified by the person's choice, as in supererogatory acts and maintenance.
حق الشرع (haqq al-shar'): A legal right that belongs to the Shari'ah (i.e., the Lawgiver); such rights cannot be altered or waived by human agreement. Examples: fixed times for qada', residence in 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the fast on a vowed day count for qada' and not the vow?
A) Because the vow is invalid if it conflicts with an earlier obligation.
B) Because qada' is a right of the Shari'ah and its absolute time cannot be restricted by the servant.
C) Because the servant did not intend the vow when fasting.
D) Because the vow was not made with the proper intention.

Key Takeaway
The servant's voluntary specification cannot restrict a Shari'ah right; it only affects his own rights.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Applying the Principle: Maintenance vs. Residence in Khul'
Applying the principle of servant's vs. Shari'ah's right to the issue of khul' stipulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between haqq al-'abd and haqq al-shar' to the khul' contract, specifically to identify that residence during 'iddah cannot be waived by stipulation.

Core Idea
Residence in 'iddah is a Shari'ah right; only maintenance, a servant's right, can be waived.

In khul' (divorce initiated by the wife for compensation), spouses may stipulate conditions about post-divorce rights. Does a stipulation that the wife forfeits maintenance and residence hold? The Hanafi school, applying the principle from the previous lesson, distinguishes between the two: maintenance is the wife's personal right (haqq al-'abd), but residence is a right of the Shari'ah (haqq al-shar'). This lesson explores the evidence and reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle
From the previous lesson: the servant's authority extends only over his own rights, not over the Shari'ah's fixed rulings.
This principle will now be applied to a new scenario.
2
Identify the khul' case
In a khul' contract, the spouses stipulate that the wife has no maintenance (nafaqah) and no residence (suknah) during her 'iddah. Which stipulations are valid?
The statement from the mashayikh in the passage provides the answer.
3
Distinguish the two rights
Maintenance is considered the wife's personal right – it is in exchange for her surrender and is therefore haqq al-'abd. Residence in the marital home during 'iddah is a right of the Shari'ah, established by Quranic commands.
The Quranic verses are explicit: 'Do not turn them out of their houses' and 'Lodge them where you dwell' (al-Talaq 65:1,6).
4
Apply the rule
Since maintenance is the wife's right, she can waive it by stipulation. However, residence is a Shari'ah right; neither spouse can waive or annul it. Therefore, the stipulation dropping maintenance is valid, but the stipulation dropping residence is void.
The husband cannot evict her, and she cannot leave except for a clear immoral act.
5
Conclude the ruling
The practical outcome: the wife is entitled to residence during 'iddah regardless of any stipulation. The maintenance can be dropped. This follows from the Quranic evidence and the principle of haqq al-shar'.
This ruling is consistent across the Hanafi school.

He forbade them from turning out and forbade them from going out. So it is known that staying in the house is the right of the Shari'ah, unlike maintenance, for it is obligatory in exchange for her surrendering herself to him and as compensation for her incapacitation from earning her livelihood, as in 'al-Ma'din'.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a fundamental usul principle: the distinction between what is a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd) and what is a right of the Shari'ah (haqq al-shar'). The author begins by examining the case of a vow (nazr) to fast. If a person vows to fast a specific day (e.g., a particular Friday), that vow becomes obligatory upon him. However, if he instead fasts that day for another obligation, such as making up missed Ramadan fasts or expiation for an oath, does the fast count for the vow or the other obligation? The ruling is that it counts for the other obligation, not the vow. The reason: the Shari'ah has made the time for making up missed fasts absolute (mutlaq) – i.e., any day. The servant's specification of a particular day cannot change that absolute ruling because the time for make-up is a right of the Shari'ah. The servant has no power to restrict it. The same applies to the fast of expiation, which is also absolute. The passage then addresses a possible objection: why does the same reasoning not apply to supererogatory (nafl) fasts? After all, supererogatory fasts are also absolute in time. So if one fasts a day that he vowed for nafl, but intends something else (like make-up), why does it count for the vow? The answer: supererogatory fasting is a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd). The Shari'ah has given the servant complete discretion over such acts. Therefore, the servant's specification does affect the supererogatory: by vowing to fast a specific day, he restricts the absolute nature of supererogatory fasting in that instance. So the act is dedicated to the vow. In other words, the servant can exercise his authority over his own rights, but he cannot tamper with the Shari'ah's rights. This principle is then applied to another area: the case of khul' (divorce initiated by the wife in return for compensation). The commentators (mashayikh) state that if the spouses stipulate in the khul' that the wife will have no maintenance (nafaqah) and no residence (suknah) during her 'iddah (waiting period), the maintenance drops but the residence does not. Why? Because maintenance during 'iddah is purely the wife's right (haqq al-'abd), so she can waive it. But residence (i.e., the right to stay in the marital home during the 'iddah) is a right of the Shari'ah (haqq al-shar'), established by explicit Quranic verses (al-Talaq 65:1,6). Neither spouse has the power to annul this right. Therefore, the husband cannot evict her, and she cannot leave (except for a clear immorality). The husband's inability to evict her is a consequence of the Shari'ah's command. The passage concludes with further discussion of the proof for the residence being a Shari'ah right: the Quranic prohibitions on eviction and leaving, combined with the fact that maintenance is in exchange for the wife's surrender and is thus a private right.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): Unrestricted in time; an absolute command without temporal specification.
مقيد (muqayyad): Restricted by a condition or specification; here, the vow restricts the otherwise absolute time of supererogatory fasting.
حق العبد (haqq al-'abd): A legal right that belongs to the individual; such rights can be waived or modified by the person's choice, as in supererogatory acts and maintenance.
حق الشرع (haqq al-shar'): A legal right that belongs to the Shari'ah (i.e., the Lawgiver); such rights cannot be altered or waived by human agreement. Examples: fixed times for qada', residence in 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school maintain that the stipulation dropping residence in khul' is invalid?
A) Because residence is considered a necessary part of 'iddah like a ritual act.
B) Because the Quran explicitly commands the husband to provide residence and prohibits eviction, making it a Shari'ah right.
C) Because the wife always retains the right to stay in the house regardless of any contract.
D) Because the khul' contract is invalid if it includes any conditions about residence.

Key Takeaway
Residence during 'iddah is a Shari'ah inviolable right; stipulations to waive it are void, unlike maintenance which is a personal right.

The Principle of Jabr bi al-Mithl and Its Application in Usul al-Fiqh
Commentary on the principle of jabr bi al-mithl with application to mu'amalat dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a deficiency in a wajib act is compensated by its like (mithl) only when a legally defined mithl exists, and identify the method of istinbat used to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
Compensation by like (jabr) applies only when a legal equivalent exists; otherwise the obligation falls.

In this passage, the commentator expands on a foundational usul rule: obligations (wajibat) are divided into two types—those that can be compensated by a like (mithl) if deficiency occurs, and those that cannot. The rule is illustrated with examples from salah and tawaf, then applied to a dispute about paying a counterfeit dirham. This lesson will walk you through the derivation of the rule and its application, focusing on the dalil and the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Identify the principle: 'Compensation by like is required for deficiencies in wajib acts; if no like exists, the obligation is waived.' This is the core usul qawida.
The passage says: 'إنه ينجبر بالسهو' and 'ينجبر بالدم' – examples from salah and tawaf.
2
Examine the examples
Note the two examples: (1) Missing parts of salah (e.g., qunut, tashahhud) are compensated by sahw; (2) Impurity during tawaf is compensated by dam. These show that a legally defined mithl exists.
The mithl is defined by shari'ah, not by human reasoning.
3
Analyze the dalil
The hadith 'جَيِّدُهَا وَرَدِيتُهَا سَوَاء' is cited to prove that quality (jihad) has no independent value in ribawi goods, hence no mithl for quality. Understand its wajh al-dalalah.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but its indication is clear in the Hanafi view.
4
Follow the dispute
Contrast Abu Hanifa's position (no liability for quality) with the Sahibayn's (liability via istihsan). Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: practical consequence in mu'amalat.
The dispute is about whether quality has a mithl; Abu Hanifa says no, Sahibayn say yes by istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الواجب بحكم الأمر نوعان. ترك قراءة الفاتحة والقنوت والتشهد وتكبيرات العيدين: إنه ينجبر بالسهو. ولو طاف طواف الفرض محدثا ينجبر ذلك بالدم وهو مثل له شرعا. وعلى أن ما لا مثل له لا يقضى. هذا لو أدى زيفا مكان جيد فهلك عند القابض لا شيء له على المديون عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله خلافا لهما. لأنه وصف لا يمكن فصله عن موصوفه منفردة لا صورة ولا معنى. أما صورة فلأنها عرض يستحيل قيامها بذاتها، وأما معنى فلأن الجودة لا قيمة لها في الأموال الربوية لقوله عليه السلام: «جَيِّدُهَا وَرَدِيتُهَا سَوَاء».
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This is a ruling passage from the matn of Usul al-Shashi, expanded by the commentator with examples. The principle states: obligations are of two types based on whether a deficiency can be compensated by something similar (mithl). If compensation by a like exists (e.g., sahw for omitted parts of salah, dam for impurity in tawaf), it is required; otherwise the deficiency is waived and no substitute is due. The commentator then applies this to monetary debts: if a debtor pays a counterfeit dirham instead of a good one, and it perishes at the creditor, does the debtor owe anything? According to Abu Hanifa, the quality of 'goodness' has no like (mithl) in the category of ribawi goods, as confirmed by the hadith that good and bad are equal in terms of value. Thus, the debtor is not liable for the quality; he only owes the like of the coin in quantity. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree, employing istihsan to protect the creditor's right to quality. The Hanafi position relies on qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) and the hadith.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr): Compensation for a deficiency by performing something similar (mithl).
مثل (mithl): The legal equivalent used to compensate a deficiency, e.g., dam for impurity in tawaf, sahw for missing parts of salah.
زيف (zayf): A counterfeit or substandard dirham; a coin of lesser quality than the standard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is a debtor not liable for paying a counterfeit dirham (zayf) instead of a good one if the counterfeit perishes?
A) Because the debtor did not intend to deceive.
B) Because quality (jihad) of a dirham has no legal similitude (mithl) in ribawi goods.
C) Because the creditor accepted the payment willingly.
D) Because the counterfeit dirham is also valuable.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of jabr bi al-mithl applies only when a legal like exists; otherwise the deficiency is not compensable, as illustrated by the dispute over counterfeit dirhams.

Al-Mithl al-Sharʿī: Legal Like in Hanafi Usul al-Fiqh
Definition and illustration of legal like with Quranic proof and dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mithl al-sharʿī, state its conditions, apply it to examples from fiqh, and distinguish the Hanafi view from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
When natural likeness is impossible, the Law creates a legal substitute — but only by divine command, not analogy.

In Hanafi usul, some obligations cannot be fulfilled by a natural equivalent. For example, if someone cannot fast due to old age, he cannot substitute an act that resembles fasting in form or meaning. Yet the Law commands a ransom of food, making it legally equivalent. This is the 'legal like' (al-mithl al-sharʿī). In this lesson, we unpack its definition, conditions, and the proof that only revelation — not reason — can establish such substitutes. We also examine a dispute with the Shafi'i school on whether qiyas can play a role.

Guided Path
1
Define the term
Identify the key term al-mithl al-sharʿī from the passage. Note that it is a substitute appointed by the Law for something that has no natural like in form or meaning.
The passage stresses that similarity of one sexual organ to another is forbidden, so no natural like exists.
2
Identify the conditions for a legal like
From the legal definition dissector, extract the conditions: (1) no natural likeness exists, (2) a divine text must explicitly establish the substitution, (3) rational analogy cannot create it.
The text says 'unless the law comes with a like' — this is the exclusive gateway.
3
Examine the proof from the Quran
Locate the Quranic verse cited (2:184) and understand how it establishes the ransom as a legal like for fasting despite no rational similarity.
The verse uses the imperative 'fidyatun' to impose the substitute, making it binding.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Use the dispute map to see that Shafi'is may allow qiyas in some cases, while Hanafis strictly require a textual basis. The footnote about false testimony illustrates a related ikhtilaf.
This shows the practical impact on fiqh rulings, e.g., witness liability.
5
Apply to a new case
Consider the case of blood money (diyah) for accidental killing: the passage says it is a legal like for the soul, even though there is no similarity between money and a human being. The same principle holds.
This reinforces that the binding force of the rule comes from the text, not reason.

وهو لا يطيق الصوم.
لما لا مثل له لا صورة ولا معنى.
الشرعي، ونظيره ما قلنا : إن الفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني مثل الصوم، والدية أي الإمساك الإطعام والمال والنفس. في القتل خطأ مثل النفس مع أنه لا مشابهة بينهما .
(1) قوله: [بالشهادة الباطلة... إلخ] يعني: إذا شهد رجلان بأنه طلق امرأته بعد الدخول فحكم القاضي عليه بأداء المهر والتفريق، ثُمّ رجع الشاهدان بعد القضاء بالفرقة لم يضمنا شيئاً عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله.
(2) قوله حتى لو وطئ... آه] دفع لتوهم وهو أن وجوب في هذه الصورة للزوجة يدلّ على تقوم منافع البضع، فدفعه بقوله: «حتى... إلخ» وبيانه: أنّ وجوب العقر لا يدلّ على تقوم منفعة البضع؛ إذ لو كان العقر قيمة لمنافع البضع لكان للزوج؛ لأنّ منافع البضع مملوكة، فلمّا كان العقر للزوجة علم أنّ وجود العقر لا يدلّ على تقوم منفعة البضع؛ لأنّ العقر وجب لأجل أنّ الوطئ في غير الملك لا يخلو عن حد زاجر أو عقر جابر لاحترام المحل، كذا في كتب الأصول.
(3) قوله: [إلا إذا ورد الشرع هذا استثناء من قوله: «فأما ما لا مثل له لا صورةً ولا معنى لا يمكن إيجاب القضاء»، كذا في "المعدن".
(4) قوله: [إنّ الفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني... إلخ] هذا نظير للقضاء بمثل غير معقول، فإنّ الفدية بمقابلة الصوم لا يدركه عقل؛ إذ لا مماثلة بينهما لا صورةً؛ لأنّ الصوم عرض والفدية عين، ولا معنى؛ لأنّ الصوم تجويع النفس والفدية إشباعه، فلم يكن الفدية مثلاً قياساً، وكذا بين النفس المقتول والدية؛ لأنّ المال مملوك متبدل والآدمي مالك متبدل، فلا يتماثلان؛ إذ المالكية سمة القدرة والمملوكية سمة العجز في طرفي النقيض هذا توضيح "المعدن"، وقال فخر الإسلام في شرح "التقويم" وإذا أقام الشرع الفدية مقام الصوم يثبت المماثلة شرعاً بين الصوم والفدية، وهذه الفدية لكل يوم هو نصف صاع من بر أو دقيقه أو سويقه أو زبيب أو صاع من تمر أو شعير للشيخ الفاني الذي يعجز عن الصوم لقوله تعالى: وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مِسْكِين الآية [البقرة : ١٨٤] أي: لا يطيقونه على أن تكون كلمة «لا» مقدرةً أو تكون «الهمزة» فيه للسلب أي: يسلبون الطاقة ليدلّ على الشيخ الفاني، انتهى.
Chapter: The obligatory by command is of two types: aggravated or by wealth, or revocable after the waiting period. Sexual intercourse by false testimony concerning divorce, and not by killing a married woman, nor by intercourse, even though it is a right of the servant [the husband] and he will be held accountable for it in the Hereafter. It is guaranteed. From the substitute for sexual intercourse. So even if he has intercourse with someone's wife, he does not guarantee anything for the husband unless the law comes with a like. Because similarity of one sexual organ to another is forbidden. What the law has brought. For that which has no like in form or meaning. That it does not resemble it in form or meaning, so it becomes a legal like and must be fulfilled with the like. Because it is not valuable except in marriage. And he cannot fast. For that which has no like in form or meaning. The legal like, and its parallel is what we said: that the ransom for the elderly who cannot fast is like fasting, and the blood money in accidental killing is like the soul, even though there is no similarity between them. (1) His saying: [By false testimony...] i.e. if two men testify that he divorced his wife after consummation, and the judge rules that he must pay the mahr and they separate, then the two witnesses retract after the ruling of separation, they do not guarantee anything according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. (2) His saying: [even if he has intercourse...] This refutes the misconception that the obligation in this case to the wife indicates the value of the benefits of sexual intercourse. So he refutes it with his saying: 'even if...' and its explanation: that the obligation of the 'uqr (dower) does not indicate the value of the benefit of sexual intercourse; because if the 'uqr were a value for the benefits of sexual intercourse, it would be for the husband, because the benefits of sexual intercourse are owned by the husband. But since the 'uqr is for the wife, it is known that the existence of 'uqr does not indicate the value of the benefit of sexual intercourse; because the 'uqr is obligatory because intercourse in non-ownership is not free from a deterrent punishment or a compensatory 'uqr to respect the place, as in the books of usul. (3) His saying: [unless the law has brought] This is an exception from his saying: 'as for what has no like in form or meaning, it is impossible to impose the recompense,' as in al-Ma'dan. (4) His saying: [that the ransom for the elderly...] This is a parallel to recompense with an unintelligible like. For the ransom in exchange for fasting is not perceived by reason; because there is no similarity between them in form: fasting is an act of worship and ransom is wealth; nor in meaning: fasting is starving the soul and ransom is feeding it. So the ransom is not a like by analogy. Similarly between the killed soul and blood money: because wealth is owned and interchangeable, while the human is an owner and interchangeable, so they are not similar; because ownership is a quality of power and being owned is a quality of incapacity, which are opposites. This is the clarification of "al-Ma'dan", and Fakhr al-Islam said in the commentary of al-Taqwim: When the law establishes the ransom in place of fasting, similarity is legally established between fasting and ransom, and this ransom per day is half a sa' of wheat or its flour or its grits or raisins, or a sa' of dates or barley for the elderly who are unable to fast, due to His saying: 'And upon those who are able [to fast] a ransom of feeding a poor person' [al-Baqarah 2:184] meaning: those who are not able, assuming the word 'la' is implied, or the hamzah is for negation i.e., they lack the ability, to indicate the elderly. End.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage discusses two main issues: first, the compensation for sexual intercourse (bada') in cases like false testimony, and second, the concept of 'legal like' (mithl shar'i) where the law establishes a substitution despite no natural similitude. The author begins by stating that the obligatory by command is of two types (likely referring to types of divorce or obligations), then moves to the specific case: if someone has intercourse with a woman due to false testimony that she was divorced, he does not guarantee anything to the husband because the husband's right is only in the Hereafter. Even if someone has intercourse with another's wife, he does not compensate the husband with a similar act because that would be forbidden. However, if the law specifies a like (mithl), it is binding. The text then explains that certain things have no natural like, so compensation can only be based on legal specification, not rational analogy. Examples: the ransom (fidyah) for an elderly person unable to fast is legally considered the like of fasting, and blood money (diyah) is the like of the slain person. Although rationally there is no similarity, the law establishes this equivalence. The footnotes elaborate: in false testimony cases, witnesses are not liable; the 'uqr (dower paid for intercourse in doubt) goes to the woman, not the husband, proving that intercourse benefits are not valued property; and the ransom for the elderly is legally equivalent to fasting due to Quranic text. This illustrates the Hanafi usul principle that in matters of compensation, when natural similitude is impossible, the law may create a legal similitude by textual command.

Key Terms
المثل الشرعي (al-mithl al-sharʿī): A substitute appointed by Law for something that has no natural equivalent, such as ransom for fasting or blood money for murder.
العقر (al-ʿuqr): A compensatory payment for intercourse in a situation of doubt or invalid marriage, going to the woman, not the man.
البضع (al-buḍʿ): The sexual organ; metaphorically the right to sexual intercourse within marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the passage, why can the 'uqr (dower for doubtful intercourse) not be considered a price for the benefit of intercourse?
A) Because the 'uqr is paid only in cases of false testimony.
B) Because the 'uqr is paid to the woman, while the benefit of intercourse is owned by the husband.
C) Because the 'uqr is less than the full mahr.
D) Because the 'uqr is a punishment, not compensation.

Key Takeaway
A legal like (al-mithl al-sharʿī) is a substitute established solely by divine text when natural similitude is absent; human reasoning (qiyas) cannot create it.

Mathil Shar'i: When the Law Creates Likeness
Explains classification of wajib and legal treatment of ma la mathil lahu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the revealed law establishes a legal likeness (mathil shar'i) for something with no natural counterpart and explain the Hanafi rationale using the examples of fidyah and diyah.

Core Idea
The shari'ah can create legal equivalence between things with no natural similarity, as with fidyah and fasting.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi usul principle: some things have no natural likeness (la mathil lahu) in form or meaning, yet the law can prescribe a substitute as if they were equivalent. This expands legal reasoning beyond qiyas. The passage also examines a dispute with al-Shafi'i regarding compensatory payments and false testimony, illustrating how the concept is applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of wajib and the classification
The passage begins by stating that the wajib by command is of two types: one emphasized (with property) and one retroactive after the waiting period. Note the classification and its relevance to the discussion on the vulva (bud').
This sets the stage for the concept of compensation.
2
Understand the concept of 'ma la mathil lahu'
The text introduces things that have no likeness in form or meaning. Recognize that such things cannot be measured by qiyas, but the law can still prescribe a substitute (mathil shar'i).
Key term: la mithl lahu.
3
Analyze the examples: fidyah and diyah
Examine the examples: fidyah for the elderly unable to fast is equated to fasting, and diyah for accidental killing is equated to the soul. Note the lack of natural similarity in both form and meaning.
Fidyah: act vs. food; diyah: life vs. money.
4
Recognize the role of explicit textual evidence (Quran 2:184)
The law establishes the likeness through a revealed text. In the case of fidyah, the Quran commands it, and the interpretation (ta'wil) applies it to those who cannot fast. This shows that the likeness is not from reason but from revelation.
Dalil: Quran 2:184.
5
Apply to the dispute with Shafi'i regarding false testimony
The passage contrasts Hanafi and Shafi'i views on witness liability. The Hanafi position relies on the idea that the vulva's benefits are not property, so compensatory payment is not a valuation. This exemplifies how the concept of mathil shar'i restricts qiyas when the law does not specify a likeness.
This dispute highlights the practical consequence of the usul principle.

(۳) قوله: [إلا إذا ورد الشرع هذا استثناء من قوله: «فأما ما لا مثل له لا صورةً ولا معنى لا يمكن إيجاب القضاء، كذا في "المعدن".
(٤) قوله: [إنّ الفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني... إلخ] هذا نظير للقضاء بمثل غير معقول، فإنّ الفدية بمقابلة الصوم لا يدركه عقل؛ إذ لا مماثلة بينهما لا صورةً؛ لأنّ الصوم عرض والفدية عين، ولا معنى؛ لأنّ الصوم تجويع النفس والفدية إشباعه، فلم يكن الفدية مثلاً قياساً، وكذا بين النفس المقتول والدية؛ لأنّ المال مملوك متبدل والآدمي مالك متبدل، فلا يتماثلان؛ إذ المالكية سمة القدرة والمملوكية سمة العجز في طرفي النقيض هذا توضيح "المعدن"، وقال فخر الإسلام في شرح "التقويم" وإذا أقام الشرع الفدية مقام الصوم يثبت المماثلة شرعاً بين الصوم والفدية، وهذه الفدية لكل يوم هو نصف صاع من بر أو دقيقه أو سويقه أو زبيب أو صاعٌ من تمر أو شعير للشيخ الفاني الذي يعجز عن الصوم لقوله تعالى: وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مِسْكِين الآية [البقرة : ١٨٤] أي: لا يطيقونه على أن تكون كلمة «لا» مقدرةً أو تكون «الهمزة» فيه للسلب أي: يسلبون الطاقة ليدلّ على الشيخ الفاني، انتهى.
Chapter: The obligatory by the command is of two types: emphasized (with property) or (retroactive after the waiting period). The vulva is by false testimony regarding divorce, and not by killing the wife of another, nor by intercourse, even though it is a right of the servant and he will be held accountable for it in the hereafter. It includes... from the substitute of the vulva, even if one has intercourse with a man's wife, he does not guarantee anything to the husband unless the revealed law comes with a likeness, along with what the revealed law has brought. Because there is no likeness for it in form or meaning. That it does not resemble it in form and meaning, so it becomes a likeness for it by law, so its compensation by a likeness is obligatory due to lack of value except in marriage. And he cannot fast. Because there is no likeness for it in form or meaning. The legal, and its analogy is what we said: Indeed, the ransom for the elderly person is like fasting, and the blood money (i.e., feeding, property, and life) in accidental killing is like the soul, even though there is no similarity between them. (1) His statement: [By false testimony... etc.] means: If two men testify that he divorced his wife after consummation, and the judge rules against him to pay the dowry and separate them, then the two witnesses retract after the ruling of separation, they do not guarantee anything according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. (2) His statement: Even if he has intercourse... etc.] is to repulse a misconception, which is that the obligation in this case for the wife indicates the valuation of the benefits of the vulva. So he repulses it by his saying: 'Even... etc.' and its explanation: that the obligation of the compensatory payment does not indicate the valuation of the benefit of the vulva; for if the obligation of the compensatory payment were the value of the benefits of the vulva, it would be for the husband, because the benefits of the vulva are owned. So when the compensatory payment is for the wife, it is known that the existence of the compensatory payment does not indicate the valuation of the benefit of the vulva; because the compensatory payment was obligated for the reason that intercourse outside ownership is not free from a deterrent penalty or a compensatory payment to honor the place, as in the books of usul. (3) His statement: [Unless the revealed law comes...] This is an exception from his statement: 'As for what has no likeness in form or meaning, it is not possible to require its compensation', as in al-Ma'din. (4) His statement: [Indeed, the ransom for the elderly person... etc.] This is analogous to compensating with an unintelligible likeness, for the ransom for fasting is not grasped by reason; because there is no similarity between them in form, because fasting is an accident and ransom is a substance, nor in meaning, because fasting is starving the soul and ransom is filling it. So the ransom is not a likeness by analogy. Likewise between the killed soul and blood money; because property is owned and changeable, while the human is an owner and changeable, so they are not similar; because ownership is a mark of ability and being owned is a mark of incapability at opposite ends. This is an explanation of al-Ma'din. Fakhr al-Islam said in Sharh al-Taqwim: When the law establishes the ransom in place of fasting, it establishes legal similarity between fasting and ransom. And this ransom for each day is half a sa' of wheat or its flour or grits or raisins, or a sa' of dates or barley for the elderly person who is unable to fast, due to His statement, the Exalted: 'And upon those who are able to fast, a ransom of feeding a poor person' [al-Baqarah 2:184] i.e., they are not able, on the condition that the word 'la' is implied, or the 'hamza' in it is for negation, i.e., they are devoid of ability, to indicate the elderly person, end.

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi discusses the classification of the obligatory (wajib) based on the command and then delves into a nuanced discussion about things that have no natural likeness (ma la mathil lahu). The author begins by stating that the obligatory is of two types: one that is emphasized (mughtallaz) typically requiring property payment, and another that is retroactive after the waiting period. He then addresses the specific case of the vulva (bud') — the wife's right to sexual intercourse — which is considered a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd) and thus punishable in the afterlife. The text clarifies that if a man has intercourse with another's wife, he does not owe any guarantee to the husband unless the revealed law prescribes a specific like for it. This introduces the concept that some things have no likeness in form or meaning, yet the law may establish a legal likeness for them. The author provides examples: the ransom (fidyah) for an elderly person unable to fast is equated to fasting, and the blood money (diyah) for accidental killing is equated to the soul, despite no apparent similarity. The footnotes elaborate on the ruling regarding false testimony and divorce, explaining the Hanafi position against al-Shafi'i, and the reasoning that the compensatory payment for intercourse outside marriage is not a valuation of the vulva's benefits but a deterrent or remedial payment. Finally, the discussion on fidyah cites the Quran and explains the legal methodology by which the law creates similarity between unlike things.

Key Terms
مثل شرعي (mathil shar'i): A legal likeness established by the revealed law for something that has no natural likeness in form or meaning.
لا مثل له (la mithl lahu): Something that has no counterpart in form or meaning, such that it cannot be measured by qiyas.
عقر (uqr): A compensatory payment for intercourse outside of marriage, given to the woman, not a valuation of benefit.

Key Takeaway
The revealed law can create legal equivalence between things that have no natural resemblance, as demonstrated by fidyah for fasting and diyah for life.

Prohibition of Legal Actions Implies Continuance of Legality
Explaining the principle and responding to objections from marriage and sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle 'al-nahy yufīd baqā' al-mashrū'iyya' to distinguish between cases where prohibition negates legality versus cases where the act remains valid in origin.

Core Idea
A prohibition on a legally valid act leaves the act's essential legality intact; only when prohibition and effect are mutually exclusive does the act become void.

This lesson unpacks a foundational principle of Hanafi usul: when the Lawgiver prohibits an action that is legally recognized in origin, the act does not lose its essential legality. Instead, the prohibition is understood as a contingent restriction. However, not all prohibitions work this way—some, like marrying one's father's wife, negate the act entirely. Through examples from marriage, sale, and vows, we will see how the Hanafi school consistently applies this principle and defends it against counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Articulate the principle
State the principle clearly: 'Prohibition of legal actions implies the continuance of their legality (baqā' al-mashrū'iyyah).' This means the act remains a valid category despite the prohibition.
The act must be legitimate in origin (shar'ī fī aslih).
2
Identify the objection from marriage
Recognise that certain prohibited marriages (e.g., marrying polytheist women, one's father's wife, or a woman in her waiting period) seem to be entirely invalid, contradicting the principle. Also, marriage without witnesses is negated by the hadith 'No marriage without witnesses.'
These are cases where the prohibition appears to nullify the act itself.
3
Analyze the Hanafi resolution: mutual exclusivity
In marriage, the effect of the contract is lawful intimacy (ḥall al-tamattu'), while the effect of the prohibition is unlawfulness (ḥurmat al-tamattu'). These are contradictory and cannot coexist, so the prohibition here implies negation (nafy/naskh) of the act's legality altogether, not continuance.
This is an exception to the general principle.
4
Contrast with sale
In a corrupt sale (bay' fāsid), ownership is established but disposal is prohibited. These two effects can coexist because ownership and prohibition of disposal are not contradictory. Thus, the prohibition implies the sale remains valid in origin (though forbidden).
The example of grape juice turning into wine illustrates this: ownership remains, but disposal is prohibited.
5
Apply to vows and disliked times
Prohibitions on fasting on Eid or praying at sunrise are due to descriptions (time), not the essence of the acts. Since the essence remains legitimate, vows for such acts are valid. The person may perform the act (though sinful) or break the vow and make up to avoid sin.
This shows the practical thamarah al-khilaf with Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النهي وهو الشرط الفاسد. (1) بيع ويجب نقضه باعتبار كونه حراما لغيره، وهذا بخلاف لأنه إيجاب وقبول. نکاح جواب إشكال أوردناه في الحاشية. نكاح المشركات ومنكوحة الأب ومعتدة الغير ومنكوحته ونكاح المحارم أو الموقت والمتعة والفاسد لعدم الإجازة. (2) الأبدية والوقتية. الشيء. والنكاح بغير شهود؛ لأن موجب النكاح حل التصرف وموجب النهي حرمة التصرف وهما متنافيان. أي الحل والحرمة. المذكور. حرمة التصرف فاستحال الجمع بينهما فيحمل النهي على النفي، فأما موجب أي خروجها عن المحلية. (1) قوله: [وهذا بخلاف نكاح... إلخ هذا جواب إشكال يرد على تفريع حكم البيع الفاسد أو جواب نقض يرد على الكلّي المذكور أعني: أنّ النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية يقتضي بقاء المشروعية؛ وذلك لأنّ نكاح المشركات منهي عنه بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَنكِحُواْ الْمُشْرِكَاتِ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٢١]، ونكاح منكوحة الأب منهي عنه بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَنكِحُوا مَا نَكَحَ آبَاؤُكُم﴾ [النساء: ٢٢]، والنكاح فعل شرعي مع أنه لم يبق مشروعاً أصلاً، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿حُرِّمَتْ عَلَيْكُمْ أُمَّهَاتُكُمْ﴾ [النساء: ٢٣] إلى قوله: ﴿وَالْمُحْصَنَاتُ منَ النِّسَاءِ﴾ [النساء: ٢٤] في معنى النهي عن نكاح المحارم وغيرها؛ لأنّ التحريم والنهي كلّ منها بمعنى المنع ومعتدة الغير في معنى منكوحة الغير، والنكاح بغير شهود منهي عنه لقوله عليه السلام: «لاَ نِكَاحَ إِلا بِشُهُود»؛ لأن النفي معنى النهي ولا يلزم أن لا يقع النكاح بغير شهود وقد يقع، فيلزم الخلف في كلام الشارع وهو منتف في كلامه بالاتفاق، فأجاب: بأن القول ببقاء المشروعية إنّما هو فيما أمكن إثبات الحرمة مع شرعية موجبة وهاهنا لم يكن ذلك؛ لأنّ موجب النكاح حلّ التصرف وموجب النهي حرمة التصرف وهما متنافيان، فاستحال الجمع بينهما، فيحمل النهي المذكور على النفي والنسخ والنفي لا يقتضي بقاء المشروعية؛ لأنّ بقاءها إنّما يلزم في النفي ضرورة اقتضاء تصوّر الفعل ليكون العبد مبتلى بين أن يأتي باختياره أو يترك باختياره ولا ابتلاء في النفي فافهم كذا في "الفصول". (2) قوله: [والنكاح بغير شهود] أي: لا يلزم على أصلنا المحكم الاعتراض من جهة النكاح بغير شهود؛ لأنه منفي بقوله عليه السلام: «لاَ نِكَاحَ إِلا بِشُهُود» رواه الدار قطني فكان نسخاً لا نهياً، وكان كلامنا في النهي دون النفي والنسخ، فيكون ذلك إخباراً عن عدمه، كقولك: «لا رجل في الدار» وذلك لا يوجب بقاء المشروعية، بل يوجب انتفاءها ضرورة صدق الخبر. فصل في النهي أي ثبوت الملك وحرمة التصرف البيع ثبوت الملك وموجب النهي حرمة التصرف وقد أمكن الجمع بينهما بيانية. في العين المشتري في البيع الفاسد. أي عصير العنب خمرا في ملكه. بأن يثبت الملك ويحرم التصرف أليس أنه لو تخمر العصير في ملك المسلم للمسلم فاجتمع الأثران أي الملك والحرمة. يبقى ملكه فيها ويحرم التصرف، وعلى هذا قال أصحابنا: «إذا نذر لصوم أي النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية تقتضي تقريرها. مثل النذر بصوم يوم النحر. يوم النحر وأيام التشريق يصح نذره»؛ لأنه نذر بصوم مشروع وكذلك لو أي النذر بصوم يوم النحر ولو لم يكن مشروعا لاستحال وجوده من العبد. وهو عاشر ذي الحجة وبعده يومان. أي الطلوع والغروب والاستواء. (3) نذر بالصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة يصح؛ لأنه نذر بعبادة مشروعة لما ذكرنا عن الأفعال الشرعية. أن النهي يوجب بقاء التصرف مشروعا ولهذا قلنا: لو شرع في النفل في هذه أي لأن النهي يوجب بقاء التصرف مشروعا. (1) قوله: [وقد أمكن... إلخ] أي: بين موجب البيع وهو ثبوت الملك وبين موجب النهي وهو حرمة التصرّف يعني: أن النكاح بخلاف البيع حيث يمكن فيه القول ببقاء المشروعية والعمل بحقيقة النهي؛ لأنّ البيع إنما شرع لملك العين والتحريم لا يضاده، والتحريم إنما يضاد المحل لا الملك، والحل في البيع يثبت تبعا فلا يضاده التحريم، كذا في شرح "المنار". (2) قوله لأنه نذر بصوم مشروع ولو كان الصوم في هذه الأيام غير مشروع لم يصح النذر به كما هو لسائر المعاصي، وقال زفر والشافعي: لا يصح النذر به ولا يلزمه شيء؛ لأن المنهي عنه معصية، وقال عليه السلام: «لاَ نَذْرَ فِي مَعْصِيَةِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى»، قلنا: قد تقرر أنّ النهى عن الأفعال الشرعية يقتضي يكون المنهي عنه مشروعاً بأصله قبيحاً بوصفه، فكان النذر به نذراً بما هو مشروع بأصله، وأمّا وصف المعصية فإنّما يتصل بفعله ولهذا يفتى أن يفطر في هذه الأيام ويقضي في أيام أخر ليحصل الخلوص عن المعصية، ولو صام في هذه الأيام خرج عن العهدة؛ لأنه أدّاه كما التزم، كذا في "المعدن". (3) قوله: [لأنه نذر بعبادة... إلخ] أي: يصح النذر بالصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة كما يصح النذر بالصوم في يوم النحر أي: صحيح بأصله؛ لأنه يساوي سائر الأوقات في كونها ظرفاً صالحاً للعبادة فاسد وهو أنّ الوقت منسوب إلى الشيطان كما جاءت به السنة؛ لأنّ النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم: «نهى عن الصلاة عند طلوع الشمس ودلوكها أي: زوالها وغروبها، وقال صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلّم: «لاَ يَتَحَرَّى أَحَدُكُمْ فَيُصَلِّي عِنْدَ طُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ وَلاَ عِنْدَ غُرُوبِهَا» وفي رواية قال: «إذا طلع الشمس فدعوا الصلاة حتى تبرز، وإذا غاب حاجب الشمس فدعوا الصلاة حتى تغيب، ولا تحينوا بصلاتكم طلوع الشمس ولا غروبها، فإنّها تطلع بين قرني الشيطان».
Chapter on Prohibition: and it is the invalid condition. (1) Sale: it is obligatory to nullify it because it is forbidden due to something else, and this is contrary to... because it is offer and acceptance. Marriage: answer to an objection we mentioned in the footnote. Marriage of polytheist women, marriage of one's father's wife, marriage of a woman in her waiting period, marriage of one's own wife, marriage of prohibited relatives, temporary marriage, marriage of pleasure, and invalid marriage due to lack of permission. (2) Eternal and temporary. The thing. And marriage without witnesses; because the effect of marriage is lawful enjoyment and the effect of prohibition is the prohibition of enjoyment, and they are contradictory. (That is, lawful enjoyment and prohibition.) mentioned. The prohibition of enjoyment; so combining them is impossible, so the prohibition is interpreted as negation and abrogation, and negation does not imply continuance of legality; because its continuance is only implied in negation due to the necessity of conceiving the act so that the servant is tested between doing it voluntarily or leaving it voluntarily, and there is no test in negation, so understand. Thus in 'al-Fusul'. (2) His statement: [And marriage without witnesses] meaning: there is no necessary objection to our reliable principle regarding marriage without witnesses; because it is negated by his statement, peace be upon him: 'No marriage except with witnesses' reported by al-Daraqutni, so it was abrogation not prohibition, and our discussion was about prohibition, not negation and abrogation. So that is an announcement of its non-existence, like your saying: 'There is no man in the house', and that does not necessitate the continuance of legality, but rather necessitates its non-existence due to the truthfulness of the report. Chapter on Prohibition: (i.e., the establishment of ownership and the prohibition of disposal) Sale: the establishment of ownership and the effect of prohibition is the prohibition of disposal, and it is possible to combine them. Clarifying statement. In the purchased item in an invalid sale. That is, grape juice becoming wine in his possession. Such that ownership is established and disposal is prohibited. Is it not that if grape juice becomes wine in the possession of a Muslim, for a Muslim, then the two effects (ownership and prohibition) combine? He retains ownership in it and disposal is prohibited. And based on this, our companions said: 'If one vows to fast (i.e., the prohibition of legal actions implies their confirmation) like the vow to fast on the day of sacrifice. The day of sacrifice and the days of tashreeq, his vow is valid'; because he vowed a valid fast. Likewise, if the vow to fast on the day of sacrifice, if it were not valid, its existence from the servant would be impossible. And it is the tenth of Dhu al-Hijjah and two days after it. (i.e., sunrise, sunset, and midday). (3) A vow to pray at disliked times is valid; because he vowed a valid worship due to what we mentioned about legal actions. That prohibition necessitates that the action remains valid. For this reason we said: if he begins a supererogatory prayer at these times, (i.e., because prohibition necessitates that the action remains valid). (1) His statement: [And it is possible... etc.] meaning: between the effect of sale (which is establishment of ownership) and the effect of prohibition (which is prohibition of disposal) — meaning that marriage is unlike sale because in sale it is possible to say that legality continues and the prohibition operates; because sale is legislated for ownership of the essence and prohibition does not contradict it, and prohibition only contradicts the place of disposal, not ownership, and lawfulness in sale is established secondarily, so prohibition does not contradict it. Thus in the commentary of 'al-Manar'. (2) His statement: because he vowed a valid fast, and if fasting on these days were not valid, the vow for it would not be valid, as is the case with other sins. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: the vow for it is not valid and nothing is obligatory upon him; because the prohibited thing is a sin, and he (peace be upon him) said: 'No vow for disobedience to Allah the Exalted.' We say: it has been established that the prohibition of legal actions implies that the prohibited thing is legitimate in its origin, detestable in its description. So the vow for it is a vow for something that is legitimate in its origin. As for the description of sin, it is only attached to its actual performance. For this reason, it is fatwa that he should break his fast on these days and make it up on other days to achieve freedom from sin. And if he fasts on these days, he fulfills his obligation; because he performed it as he committed to it. Thus in 'al-Ma'din'. (3) His statement: [because he vowed a worship... etc.] meaning: a vow to pray at disliked times is valid just as a vow to fast on the day of sacrifice is valid. That is, it is valid in its origin; because it is equal to other times in being a suitable container for worship. It is corrupt that the time is associated with Satan as reported in the Sunnah; because the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, forbade prayer at sunrise, at its decline (i.e., its zenith) and at sunset. And he, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Let none of you deliberately pray at sunrise nor at sunset.' And in a narration: 'When the sun rises, leave prayer until it is fully up; and when the disc of the sun sets, leave prayer until it is fully set. And do not time your prayers at sunrise or sunset, for it rises between the two horns of Satan.'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the usul principle: 'Prohibition of legal actions implies the continuance of their legality.' The author (the commentator) first raises an objection: if this principle holds, then why are certain marriages (like marrying polytheist women or one's father's wife) prohibited and yet not considered valid acts? The answer is that in marriage, the effect of marriage (lawful intimacy) and the effect of prohibition (unlawfulness of intimacy) are mutually exclusive, so combining them is impossible. Therefore, the prohibition in such cases is interpreted as negation (naskh/abrogation) of the act's legality altogether, not as a prohibition of an otherwise valid act. The example of marriage without witnesses is similarly explained: the hadith 'No marriage except with witnesses' is a negation (khabar) meaning the act is invalid, not a prohibition that implies legality. The passage then contrasts marriage with sale: in a corrupt sale (bay' fāsid), ownership is established while disposal is prohibited, and these two effects can coexist because ownership and prohibition are not contradictory. The same logic applies to the example of grape juice turning into wine—ownership remains but disposal is prohibited. The commentary then applies the principle to vows (nudhūr): a vow to fast on the day of Eid (which is prohibited) is valid according to the Hanafis because the act of fasting is legitimate in its origin; the prohibition is due to a description (time) that can be overcome (by making up the fast). The author reports that Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree, citing the hadith 'No vow in disobedience to Allah,' but the Hanafi response is that the prohibition attaches to the performance, not the essence. Similarly, a vow to pray at disliked times is valid because the prayer itself is a valid act; the dislike is due to external factors (time), not the act's essence. The author provides hadith evidence for the disliked times. The passage thus consistently defends the Hanafi usul principle and refutes counterexamples.

Key Terms
النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية (al-nahy 'an al-af'āl al-shar'iyyah): Prohibition of actions that are legally recognized as acts of worship or transactions; this indicates the act is legitimate in origin but prohibited due to an external factor.
النفي والنسخ (al-nafy wa al-naskh): Negation and abrogation — used when combining the act's effect and prohibition is impossible; the prohibition is interpreted as nullifying the act's legality.
المشروعية (al-mashrū'iyyah): Legality in origin — the act is recognized by the Lawgiver as a valid category of action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle discussed, why is a vow to fast on the day of Eid valid despite the prohibition of fasting on that day?
A) Because the prohibition is abrogated for vows.
B) Because the act of fasting is legitimate in its origin; the prohibition is due to a description.
C) Because the Prophet permitted vows on prohibited days.
D) Because Eid fasting is only disliked, not forbidden.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that prohibition implies continued legality requires distinguishing between prohibitions that contradict the act's essence and those that leave the essence intact.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
The Principle of al-Nahy: Prohibition Does Not Invalidate Legal Effects
Explains the principle that prohibition due to external reason does not invalidate legal effects, with multiple fiqh examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi distinction between nahy li-dhātihi and nahy li-ghayrihi, and apply it to determine whether a prohibited act still produces legal effects.

Core Idea
Prohibition for an external reason does not negate the legal existence of an act.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces a foundational Hanafi principle: the prohibition (nahy) of an act does not necessarily render it legally invalid. The key is whether the prohibition targets the essence of the act or an external attribute. Using the example of intercourse during menstruation (Quran 2:222), the author demonstrates that even prohibited acts can produce legal effects such as ihsan, mahr, and iddah. This lesson will guide you through the distinction and its application across various fiqh cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Prohibition and Its Reason
Find the act that is prohibited (intercourse with a menstruating woman) and the reason given for the prohibition (harm, not the act's essence).
The verse says 'do not approach them until they are pure'—the prohibition is due to menstruation, not intercourse itself.
2
Distinguish Essence vs. External Attribute
Determine whether the prohibition targets the act's intrinsic nature (nahy li-dhātihi) or an external description (nahy li-ghayrihi). In this case, the act's essence (intercourse) is not inherently prohibited; the prohibition is for an external condition (menstruation).
If the prohibition were on the essence, the act would be invalid (bāṭil).
3
Apply to the Example of Menstruation
List the legal effects that still occur despite the prohibition: ihsan (status of muhsan), mahr, iddah, nafaqah. Understand why these effects attach: the act is legally existent.
The Hanafi position is that ihsan is established even if intercourse is haram.
4
Generalize to Other Fiqh Cases
Examine the additional examples in the text: divorce during menstruation, ablution with stolen water, hunting with a borrowed bow, prayer in usurped land, selling during the call to prayer. For each, verify that the prohibition is external and that legal effects remain valid.
The common thread: the prohibition is due to an external harm or violation of rights.
5
Understand the Shafi'i Counterargument and Hanafi Response
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that a haram act cannot be a cause for divine blessings (legal effects). The Hanafi response: the prohibition is not on the act's essence, so the act remains valid and produces effects; blessings are not nullified.
This leads to the practical difference regarding testimony of a fasiq.

فصل في النهي
أي كون وطي الحائض منهيا بمعنى الأذى لالعينه. وَلَا تَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حتّى يَطْهُرْنَ ﴾ [البقرة: ۲۲۲]، ولهذا قلنا: يترتب الأحكام على أي وطئ الحائض. المطلقة ثلاثا. بوطى الحائض. هذا الوطئ فيثبت به إحصان الواطئ وتحل المرأة للزوج الأول ويثبت به فتعد بالطلاق لكونها موطوعة. أي إقدارها إياه على جماعها وإعطاها إياه مكان وطيها. حكم "المهر والعدة والنفقة، ولو امتنعت عن التمكين لأجل الصداق (o) لا تستحق النفقة والسكني فيجب لأنها سلمت إليه بضعها. د أبي حنيفة تستحق النفقة. عند أي المهر. كانت ناشزة عندهما فلا تستحق النفقة، وحرمة الفعل لا تنـــافي ترتــب أحكام الوجود والصحة. (۷) (۸) فيترتب عليه إباحة الصلاة ومس المصحف. الأحكام كطلاق الحائض والوضوء بالمياه المغصوبة وإن كان ممنوعا فإنه يقع.
(۱) قوله: [يترتب الأحكام وإنما يترتب الأحكام المشروعة على هذا الوطئ؛ لأنه غير منهي لمعنى في عينه، بل لمعنى في غيره.
(۲) قوله: [إحصان الواطي] أي: للرجم وإحصان الرجم أن يكون حراً عاقلاً بالغاً مسلماً، قد دخل بامرأة دخولاً حلالاً، فإذا وطئ الحائض كان محصناً كما إذا وطئ غير الحائض حتى لو زنى بعد ذلك كـــان حده الرجم دون الجلد، كذا في "المعدن".
(۳) قوله: [وتحل... إلخ] يعني: إذا كانت المرأة مطلقةً بثلاث تطليقات فتزوجها زوج آخر ودخل بهــــا في حالة الحيض وطلّقها تحلّ للزوج الأوّل.
(٤) قوله: [حكم المهر ... إلخ فإنّه يجب المهر على الزوج بهذا الوطئ كاملاً وكذا تجب العدّة بهذا الوطئ لو طلقها وكذا تجب النفقة بهذه العدّة.
(٥) قوله: [كانت ناشزةً عندهما وعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تستحق النفقة كما لو امتنعت بعد ما وطئت في غير حالة الحيض عن التمكن بالوطئ، وإنما تترتّب الأحكام المشروعة على هذا الوطئ؛ لأنه غــــير نهي لممعنى في عينه.
(٦) قوله: [وحرمة الفعل... إلخ] جواب سؤال مقدّر وهو أن يقال: إنّ الوطئ في هذه الصورة حرام فلا يصلح أن يكون سبباً للأحكام المشروعة؛ إذ الحكم المشروع نعمة وكرامة، فلا ينال بما هو حرام ومعصية كما قال الشافعي رحمه الله، فأجاب : بأنّ حرمة الفعل لا تنافي ثبوت الأحكام شرعاً، كذا في "المعدن".
(۷) قوله : [ كطلاق الحائض] فإنّه وإن كان حراماً لكنه يترتب عليه الأحكام الشرعية من الحرمة والفرقـــة العدّة وغيرها من الأحكام كسائر الطلاقات شرعا.
(۸) قوله: [والوضوء... إلخ فإنّ التوضي بها كان حراماً، لكنّه يترتب عليها الأحكام الشرعية من إباحة الصلاة ومس المصحف وغيرهما.
وإن كان الاصطياد بها حراما لكن يحل الصيد. (1) (۲) فيحل المذبوح. فيفرغ ذمة المصلي. والاصطياد بقوس مغصوبة والذبح بسكين مغصوبة والصلاة في الأرض فإنه حرام لغيره لكنه يترتب عليه أحكامه من الملك و صحة التصرف فيه وغيرهما. المذكورة. المغصوبة والبيع في وقت النداء فإنّه يترتب الحكم على هذه التصرفات مع أي التصرفات. (°) وهو أن التهي لا ينفي الوجود المشروعية. اشتمالها على الحرمة، وباعتبار هذا الأصل قلنا في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْبَلُوا (۷) أي صالح لها في نفسه. شَهَادَةً أَبَداً [النور: ٤] ، إن الفاسق من أهل الشهادة فينعقد النكـــاح أي المحدودين في القذف.
(۱) قوله: [بقوس... إلخ] فإنّ الاصطياد بهذا القوس وإن كان حراماً، لكنه يترتب عليه الأحكام الشرعية من حلّ الصيد وإفادة الملك وغيرهما، كذا في "المعدن".
(۲) قوله: [ والذبح... إلخ] فإنّ الذبح بهذا السكين وإن كان حراماً، لكنه يترتب عليه حلّ المذبوح وغيره.
(۳) قوله: [والصلاة... إلخ ] فإنّ الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة وإن كان حراماً لكنه يترتب عليهـــا جــــواز الصلاة، لكنها تكره بقبح جاوره، فإن قيل: ما الفرق بين وطئ الحائض والصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة في كون الأوّل حراماً والثاني مكروهاً مع كون المعنى الذي هو قبيح لأجله وهو الأذى وشغل حق الغير مجاوراً، قيل: القياس يقتضي كراهية وطئ الحائض إلاّ أنّ الحرمة ثابتة بالإجماع أو بالنص على خلاف القياس، كذا في كتب الأصول.
(٤) قوله: [والبيع... إلخ فإنّ البيع في هذا الوقت وإن كان منهيًّا عنه، لكنه يفيد الملك وغيره من أحكام البيع، ثُمّ الحرمة في الأمور المذكورة لاشتمالها على شغل ملك الغير واستعماله بدون الإذن، فافهم ولا تزل، كذا قيل.
(٥) قوله وباعتبار] أي: أنّ حرمة الفعل لا تنافي ثبوت الأحكام أو باعتبار أن النهي عن التصرفات الشرعيّة يقتضي بقاء المشروعية كذا في "المعدن".
(٦) قوله: [إنّ الفاسق من أهل الشهادة أورد عليه أن تفريع هذه المسألة على أنّ النهي يقتضي بقــاء المشروعية مشكل، فإنّ المنهي عنه هو قبول الشهادة، فهذا الأصل يقتضي أن يكون قبول شهادتهم مشروعاً بأصله غير مشروع بوصفه، فينبغي أن ينعقد قبولها لثبوت حق الغير له، إما أن يكون قبولهــــا باطلاً من كُلّ، ولا يكون شهادة بأصلها فليس من مقتضيات هذا الأصل، وأجيب عنه: بأن قبـول الشهادة وعدَمه يستلزم أهليّة الشهادة فكان النهي عن قبول الشهادة نهيا عن أهليتها وهو يقتضي تقرير أهليته للشهادة فيكون هذه المسألة من مقتضيات هذا الأصل بهذا الطريق، كذا في "الملتقط".
(۷) قوله: [من أهل الشهادة... آه] ولقائل أن يقول: إنّ الشهادة هو إخبار عن علم وذلك متصوّر مـــــن الفاسق ولا يلزم من ذلك أهلية الشهادة حتى يكون الفاسق من أهلها وإلا لزم أن يكون الصبي والعبد
Chapter on Prohibition. That is, the prohibition of sexual intercourse with a menstruating woman is in the sense of harm, not in its essence. And do not approach them until they are pure. [Quran 2:222] And for this we said: Legal effects are established upon it. ... And the prohibition of the act does not negate the establishment of the rulings of existence and validity. ... And the prohibition of the act does not preclude the legal effects. ... And based on this principle we said regarding the verse 'And do not accept their testimony ever' [Quran 24:4]: Indeed the sinful person is among those qualified for testimony, so the marriage contract is valid.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the principle that prohibition (نهي) on an act does not necessarily invalidate its legal consequences, when the prohibition is due to an external factor and not the act's essence. The text begins with the example of sexual intercourse with a menstruating woman, citing Quran 2:222. Although such intercourse is forbidden, it still produces legal effects: it establishes ihsan (status of being muhsan), it makes the woman lawful for her first husband if she was divorced three times and then married another, it imposes mahr, iddah, and nafaqah. The commentator notes that Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the woman still deserves nafaqah even if she is nashiz (rebellious), unlike the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The key point: the prohibition is not on the act itself but on its timing or context. The text then generalizes to other examples: divorce during menstruation, ablution with stolen water, hunting with a borrowed bow, slaughtering with a borrowed knife, prayer in usurped land, and selling during the call to prayer — all are forbidden but their legal effects remain valid. This is because the prohibition is for an external reason (darar, ghasp, etc.). The text then applies this to the issue of testimony of a fasiq: although the Quran forbids accepting their testimony, the fasiq is still capable of giving testimony (ahl al-shahada), and a marriage contract witnessed by them is valid. The discussion includes a rebuttal to Imam al-Shafi'i's argument that a forbidden act cannot be a cause for divine blessings. The Hanafi response is that the prohibition does not negate legal existence.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A shari' prohibition that may be on the act itself (ذاتي) or on an external attribute (غير ذاتي); the distinction determines whether the act remains valid
الفاسق (al-fāsiq): A person who commits a major sin; such a person is considered ahl al-shahāda (capable of giving testimony) in Hanafi law, though their testimony is not accepted due to prohibition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle discussed, if a person prays in a stolen garment (thawb maghsūb), what is the ruling on the prayer?
A) The prayer is invalid because the garment is stolen.
B) The prayer is valid but sinful, and it fulfills the obligation.
C) The prayer is nullified and must be repeated.
D) The prayer is permissible without sin because necessity overrides.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between nahy li-dhātihi and nahy li-ghayrihi preserves the validity of acts whose prohibition stems from external factors, ensuring legal effects remain intact.
